People v. Rodriguez


No. B088842


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN


47 Cal. App. 4th 424; 54 Cal. Rptr. 2d 801; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 662; 96 Daily Journal DAR 8497; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5283



July 16, 1996, Decided  
July 16, 1996


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted October 23, 1996 (S055670), Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 5974. (See 17 Cal. 4th 253.)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BA094493. Hon. Bernard J. Kamins, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction, entered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), for possessing for sale cocaine base, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code §11350.5, contending that the evidence that he possessed cocaine base was insufficient, and that the trial court erred with respect to rulings on "strikes."


OVERVIEW: Appellant was arrested after police observed him in a narcotic transaction. The police retrieved a baggie containing 15 rocks of cocaine, which appellant had dropped. Appellant was charged with possessing for sale cocaine base, and multiple prior felonies were alleged, including four "strikes." The allegations were bifurcated, the jury was waived, and the trial court found three of the allegations true, and sentenced appellant to prison for 25 years to life. On appeal, the court affirmed, remanding only to allow the trial court to determine whether or not to exercise its limited discretion to dismiss a "strike," which the trial court was not aware it had discretion to do at appellant's sentencing. The court found that an evidentiary gap in the proof of whether appellant possessed cocaine base was closed by substantial evidence from an exhibit, admitted into evidence, and viewed by the jury, which contained not only the remaining rocks of cocaine but also the results of two tests performed by a chemist that were included in the chemist's admitted report. Further, the alleged "strike" of a conviction of assault with a deadly weapon was supported by substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for possessing for sale cocaine base based on sufficient evidence, but remanded to allow the trial court to decide whether to exercise its discretion to dismiss a "strike," which the trial court was unaware it had discretion to do at appellant's sentencing.

 

669 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Tran


No. H014136.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


47 Cal. App. 4th 253; 54 Cal. Rptr. 2d 650; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 659; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5126; 96 Daily Journal DAR 8225



July 8, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified August 7, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Santa Clara County Superior Court. Super. Ct. No. 178779. Hon. Alden E. Danner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Santa Clara County Superior Court (California), which convicted him of felony stalking and assault with a deadly weapon. Defendant contended that Cal. Penal Code § 646.9, pertaining to the crime of stalking, was unconstitutionally vague and therefore violative of his right to due process. He also contended there was insufficient evidence to support his conviction of an assault against an infant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had repeatedly stalked and threatened harm to a female acquaintance and her family because he wanted to be romantically involved with her. On the night of his arrest he chased the acquaintance's husband with a knife while the husband was carrying his infant son. Defendant was convicted of one count of felony stalking and two counts of assault with a deadly weapon. Defendant contended that the element in Cal. Penal Code § 646.9, a stalking statute, requiring that defined objectionable conduct "serve no legitimate purpose" was unconstitutionally vague. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court held that the terms "serve no legitimate purpose" read in context was comprehensible and that the statute prohibited certain, described conduct such that an ordinary person reasonably could understand what conduct was expressly prohibited. As to defendant's assertion that there was insufficient evidence to support an assault conviction on the infant, the court held that defendant's contention that he did not intend to harm the infant was without merit. All that was required to be proved was that he willfully pursued father and son with a knife.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant for felony stalking and assault with a deadly weapon. The court held that the state's stalking statute proscribed certain, described conduct such that an ordinary person reasonably could understand what conduct was expressly prohibited. The court held that where defendant chased an individual holding an infant with a knife, it was of no merit that he intended no harm to the baby.

 

670 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Pro-Family Advocates v. Gomez


No. A070532.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


46 Cal. App. 4th 1674; 54 Cal. Rptr. 2d 600; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 641; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4962; 96 Daily Journal DAR 7921



July 1, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Marin County Superior Court. Super. Ct. No. 162051. Hon. Peter Allen Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order modifying the preliminary injunction is reversed and the matter is remanded for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant director of the California Department of Corrections sought review of the decision by the Marin County Superior Court (California), which issued a preliminary injunction barring enforcement of an administration regulation excluding certain prisoners from participation in the overnight family visiting program.


OVERVIEW: Respondent pro-family advocates filed suit against appellant director of the California Department of Corrections seeking to enjoin appellant from enforcing an administrative regulation that excluded certain prisoners from participating in the overnight family visiting program. The court reversed the trial court's preliminary injunction and held that the regulation did not constitute an ex post facto law, pursuant to U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, cl. 1, nor did it violate U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court concluded the regulation did not constitute punishment within the meaning of the ex post facto clauses and although the regulation had a punitive effect and one of its purposes was penal, it also had a legitimate, nonpunitive governmental purpose of maintaining institutional security. The court reason that since family visits may be totally banned, the department necessarily had the authority to restrict participation in the program. The court concluded that the regulation had a reasonable basis and its classification were rationally related to the legitimate state purpose of institutional security, and as such the regulation did not violate equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's preliminary injunction against appellant director of the California Department of Corrections that barred the enforcement of an administration regulation excluding certain prisoners from participating in an overnight family visiting program. The court concluded the regulations did not constitute an ex post facto law nor did the regulations violate equal protection.

People v. Soto


No. B092155.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN


46 Cal. App. 4th 1596; 54 Cal. Rptr. 2d 593; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 642; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4967; 96 Daily Journal DAR 7952



June 28, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order of the Los Angeles County Superior Court. Super. Ct. No. BA104038. Hon. Arthur M. Lew, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the matter remanded to the superior court with directions to permit defendant to withdraw his plea of guilty should he choose to do so within 30 days following finality of our decision and for such further proceedings as may be required.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff people appealed an order of the Los Angeles County Superior Court (California), which struck the allegation of defendant accused's prior robbery conviction, a "strike" under California's "three strikes" law, Cal. Penal Code §667(b)-(i), in defendant's charge of one count of possession of cocaine.


OVERVIEW: Defendant accused made a pretrial motion to dismiss an allegation of a prior conviction being considered pursuant to California's "three strikes law," Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)-(i), after he was charged with possession of cocaine. Defendant contended that his prior conviction resulted from an invalid waiver of his right to a jury trial where the judge mistakenly stated that defendant's lawyers would decide his guilt or innocence at trial. The trial court struck the allegation, from which plaintiff people appealed. On appeal, the court reversed the order because defendant failed to meet his burden of establishing that he improperly waived his right to a jury trial. The court held that if defendant was confused by the trial court's explanation of a jury trial, he was required to allege and prove his confusion. The court observed that state court defendants in noncapital cases were permitted to file a pretrial motion to strike prior convictions despite a federal decision abolishing that remedy as the federal decision did not apply to state courts. Thus, the court reversed and remanded, ordering that defendant be given an opportunity to withdraw his guilty plea as to the cocaine charge.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order striking plaintiff people's allegation of defendant accused's prior conviction because defendant failed to meet his burden of proving that the prior conviction was invalid by virtue of an improper waiver of the right to a jury trial where defendant merely made conclusory allegations. The court remanded with directions to permit defendant to withdraw his plea of guilty should he choose to do so.

In re Marriage of Ousterman


No. A070817.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


46 Cal. App. 4th 1090; 54 Cal. Rptr. 2d 403; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 591; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4730; 96 Daily Journal DAR 7493



June 25, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Alameda County Superior Court. Super. Ct. No. 497387-6. Hon. Roderic Duncan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed and the matter remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband challenged a ruling by the Alameda County Superior Court (California), that it had jurisdiction under Cal. Fam. Code § 4336 to consider a motion for modification of spousal support filed by respondent wife.


OVERVIEW: Appellant husband challenged the trial court's decision to exercise jurisdiction under Cal. Fam. Code. § 4336 over a motion for modification of spousal support filed by respondent wife after the last date the original order required support to be paid. The court affirmed, concluding that the trial court had jurisdiction to proceed. The parties' 25-year marriage was dissolved in 1978; a stipulated support order filed in 1986 did not contain language expressly terminating support at a stated time but rather provided for a period of support and prohibited respondent from modifying its terms "on or before" the end of that period. The court held that the lack of a specifically demonstrated intention to preclude modification required a finding that the agreement was in fact modifiable after the specified date and that the trial court properly found jurisdiction under § 4336.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and remanded to the trial court. The court determined that, in the absence of any clear intention that the spousal support order could not be modified, the order was modifiable, and the trial court retained jurisdiction under the current statute to entertain respondent wife's motion for modification over the objections of appellant husband.

People v. Haywood


No. E010654


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


46 Cal. App. 4th 569; 54 Cal. Rptr. 2d 120; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 571; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4373; 96 Daily Journal DAR 6991



June 17, 1996, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified July 11, 1996. Review Denied September 25, 1996 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 5576.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County. Super. Ct. No. SCR 54931. Hon. Patrick J. Morris, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) convicting him of possession of a firearm by a felon in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12021(a). The matter came before the court after the California Supreme Court directed the court to vacate its previous decision affirming defendant's conviction and to reconsider the cause.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession of a firearm by a felon in violation of Cal. Penal. Code § 12021(a). Defendant argued that the trial court erred in instructing the jury that his prior conviction was a felony because the court's instructions removed determination of an essential element of the charged offense from the jury's consideration. The court affirmed defendant's conviction, but the state supreme court vacated the court's opinion. On reconsideration, the court again affirmed, finding that the trial court misleadingly and erroneously allowed evidence on the preliminary legal question of the felony status of defendant's prior crime to be presented before the jury. However, the court also found that the trial court did ultimately and properly determine the preliminary question of the felony status of the prior conviction itself, and that it also properly instructed on the legal status of that conviction as a felony. The court held that the instructions did not remove an element of the crime from decision by the jury, as the jury was called upon only to decide whether the felony conviction offered was in fact suffered by defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant of possession of a firearm by a felon because it concluded that the trial court's instruction that defendant's prior conviction was for a felony did not remove an essential element of the charged offense from the jury's determination.

In re Marriage of Rebecca & David R.


No. E013860


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


46 Cal. App. 4th 431; 53 Cal. Rptr. 2d 870; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 552; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4306; 96 Daily Journal DAR 6915



June 14, 1996, Filed


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Granted July 1, 1996. Opinion of April 21, 1997, Reported at: 54 Cal. App. 4th 471, 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 309.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of Riverside County. Super. Ct. No. D-121563. Hon. Richard G. Van Frank, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former husband sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which issued a judgment in favor of respondent former wife that ordered appellant to pay continuing child support for two children born during the parties' marriage who were not his biological children, pursuant to Cal. Fam. Code § 7540.


OVERVIEW: Appellant former husband sought to end his child support obligation to respondent former wife and their two minor children. During the course of dissolution proceedings, the court ordered blood tests to establish paternity, and appellant learned that he was not the biological father of either child. Appellant had acted as father to the children, while the biological father had not. The trial court denied appellant's request to terminate his obligation, pursuant to the presumption of parentage in Cal. Fam. Code § 7540. On appeal, the court affirmed, and held that although the order for the blood tests did not occur until more than two years after the birth of the youngest child, the two-year statute of limitation provided in § 7541(b) was applicable only to motions for blood tests brought by a husband, and not to those ordered by a court. The court also held that appellant had no constitutionally protectable interest in severing the parent/child relationship because the interest of the state in the welfare of the children, in promoting marriage, and not undermining the family unit, would not be served by allowing appellant to end his parent/child relationship with the children.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which ordered appellant former husband to continue making child support payments to appellee former wife and their two minor children. The court held that the two-year statute of limitation did not preclude a court from ordering a blood test to determine parentage, and that the interests of the state in the welfare of the children overrode those of the husband in terminating his obligation.

People v. Ayon


No. D024062.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


46 Cal. App. 4th 385; 53 Cal. Rptr. 2d 853; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 555; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4273; 96 Daily Journal DAR 6863



June 13, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County. Super. Ct. No. SCD109757. Hon. Melinda J. Lasater, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The sentence in this case is reversed. The trial court is directed to impose a new sentence, consisting of an indeterminate term of 225 years to life, plus three 5-year enhancements or 15 years for the prior serious felony convictions under section 667, subdivision (a)(1). The trial court is directed to prepare an amended abstract of judgment reflecting such changes in the sentence and to forward it to the Department of Corrections. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), and claimed that his 225 years to life sentence was calculated incorrectly under Cal. Penal Code § 1170.12(c)(2)(A)(iii). Defendant claimed that his sentence should have been calculated according to §1170.12(c)(2)(A)(i), which would have amounted to a sentence of 48 years to life.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced to 225 years to life for his 7 convictions of robbery and 2 convictions of attempted robberies. Defendant claimed that the trial court erred in calculating his sentence. The court held that under Cal. Penal Code § 1170.12(c)(2)(A), a three strikes defendant was to be sentenced to the greater of three options as the minimum term and the sentences were to run consecutively. Thus, under § 1170(c)(2)(A)(iii), determinate sentencing rules were properly applied singularly to each current conviction and the aggregate term was not to be calculated. Further, multiple current felony convictions were to be combined, as opposed to considered separately, when calculating the mandatory minimum term. The court held, however, that defendant's current felonies were serious and he had three prior serious felony convictions under Cal. Penal Code § 667(a)(1), and a life prior under Cal. Penal Code § 1170.12(b) and (c), and thus the trial court was required to impose three five year enhancements. The court reversed the trial court's sentence as an unauthorized sentence, and directed the court to impose a new sentence consisting of an indeterminate term of 240 years to life.


OUTCOME: The court found defendant's claims without merit because his calculations were based on a faulty premise and inapplicable statute. However, the court reversed his sentence as an unauthorized sentence, and directed the trial court to impose a new sentence, consisting of an indeterminate term of 225 years to life, plus three 5-year enhancements for defendant's prior serious felony convictions. In all other respects, the judgment was affirmed.

People v. Bostick


No. A067626.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


46 Cal. App. 4th 287; 53 Cal. Rptr. 2d 760; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 535; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4075; 96 Daily Journal DAR 6575



June 6, 1996, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part I.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied September 4, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 5082.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court County of Contra Costa. Super. Ct. No. 940597-8. Hon. Wayne A. Westover, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction in the Superior Court County of Contra Costa (California), for voluntary manslaughter with a special finding that he discharged a firearm from a motor vehicle causing another's death within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 12022.55.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury of voluntary manslaughter, with a special finding that he discharged a firearm from a motor vehicle causing another's death, within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 12022.55. Defendant sought reversal on the grounds that the jury should have been instructed that the gun enhancement statute contained an implied "drive-by" element. The court affirmed the judgment because § 12022.55 was plain on its face that all that was required was that death or great bodily injury be inflicted as a result of discharging a firearm from a motor vehicle. The court noted that the term "drive-by" did not appear anywhere and that defendant cited no language which remotely suggested that the vehicle must be in motion for the enhancement to apply. The court further held that defendant's argument would fail even if the court independently construed the legislature's intent, because although the court had no doubt that the gang-type drive-by shooting was one of the primary concerns in enacting § 12022.55, there were other plausible purposes for the enhancement. namely, deterring persons from violent actions upon our public streets and highways.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment because the statute was plain on its face that all that was required was that death or great bodily injury be inflicted as a result of discharging a firearm from a motor vehicle. The court noted that the term "drive-by" did not appear anywhere and that defendant cited no language which remotely suggested that the vehicle must be in motion for the enhancement to apply.

 

678 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Bonner v. City of Santa Ana


No. G012674.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


45 Cal. App. 4th 1465; 53 Cal. Rptr. 2d 671; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 503; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3932; 96 Daily Journal DAR 6335



May 31, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 28, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 5046.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Super. Ct. No. 624481. Hon. Eileen C. Moore, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to enter judgment in favor of the city. In light of Bonner's having prevailed at trial, our earlier opinion giving him a chance at some recovery on his due process claim, and the fact the City of Santa Ana did not raise the dispositive point at trial, it is fitting that the parties bear their own costs on appeal. Obviously the argument in Bonner's cross-appeal that the trial court should have awarded him attorney fees falls with the loss of his two causes of action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand from the California Supreme Court, plaintiff homeless person sought to establish that his equal protection rights and due process rights under Cal. Const. art. I, § 7, had been violated by defendant city when defendant threw a bag containing plaintiff's possessions into the trash. The trial court had found for defendant. Plaintiff also sought attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff homeless person stored his possessions in a trash bag, which was thrown away by defendant city's workers. Plaintiff sought damages based upon violations of his equal protection and due process rights under Cal. Const. art. I, § 7. At trial, plaintiff prevailed and was awarded money damages. Defendant appealed, and the court reversed. Plaintiff then appealed to the state supreme court, which remanded the case to the court for further consideration. On remand, the court held that plaintiff was not entitled to money damages for a violation of the state due process clause. As there was nothing in the language or history of the state equal protection clause indicating voter intent to allow damages, plaintiff had no right to sue for money damages. As for plaintiff's due process claim, the court held plaintiff was not entitled to a direct constitutional claim or money damages. Thus, the court reversed the trial court's judgment with directions to enter judgment in favor of defendant. The court also ordered that the parties bear their own costs on appeal.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment in favor of plaintiff homeless person against defendant city because plaintiff was not entitled to money damages for a violation of the state due process clause or equal protection clause, given state case precedent and the legislative intent of state voters in ratifying the state constitution. The court further ordered that each party bear its own attorney costs.

 

679 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



J & K Painting Co. v. Bradshaw


No. A067893.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


45 Cal. App. 4th 1394; 53 Cal. Rptr. 2d 496; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 496; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3862; 96 Daily Journal DAR 6293; 132 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P58,188



May 30, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied June 21, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 601. Review Denied August 28, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 4961.


PRIOR HISTORY: San Francisco Superior Court. Super. Ct. No. 958810. Hon. Stuart R. Pollak, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Since it appears that these procedures were substantially followed here, there was no basis for issuance of a writ of mandamus. Accordingly, the judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant labor agency and plaintiff subcontractor both appealed a decision of the San Francisco Superior Court (California), which issued a writ of mandate setting aside defendant's notice to withhold underpaid wages and statutory penalties, under Cal. Lab. Code § 1720-1780, from plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Upon learning that plaintiff subcontractor paid less than the prevailing wage to its employees on a public works project, defendant labor agency issued a notice to the contracting agency directing it to withhold statutory penalties and underpaid wages, in accord with the prevailing wage law, Cal. Lab. Code § 1720-1780. On petition of plaintiff, the trial court held that the notice to withhold should not have issued until defendant labor agency had fixed the amount of the penalty. The court reversed and held that in Cal. Lab. Code § 1727, which imposed upon the contracting agency the duty to withhold the wages and penalties which had been forfeited, the term "forfeited" did not require that the agency to have first actually perfected the forfeiture. The court stated that the moment the subcontractor violated the law by paying less than the prevailing wages, it rendered itself liable for the loss of, or forfeited, the penalties and underpaid wages. The court held that the awarding agency's power and duty to withhold penalties were thus predicated on a preliminary or tentative estimation of penalties.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment setting aside the notice to withhold penalties and wages from plaintiff subcontractor, rendering moot plaintiff's appeal, finding that notice was proper because under the statute, defendant labor agency was not required to have first fixed the penalty before issuing an order to withhold underpaid wages and penalties.

 

680 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Freeman


No. D018779.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


45 Cal. App. 4th 1437; 53 Cal. Rptr. 2d 439; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 502; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3939; 96 Daily Journal DAR 6271



May 30, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment and an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County. Super. Ct. No. D321746. Hon. Thomas Ashworth III, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment and order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former husband challenged a child support order from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which presumed that appellant, who was infertile but not sterile, was the father of the child, under Cal. Fam. Code § 7540.


OVERVIEW: Appellant former husband was infertile, but not absolutely sterile, when respondent former wife had unprotected sexual relations with another man and became pregnant. After expiration of the statute of limitations for filing a notice of motion for blood tests, appellant filed for dissolution of the marriage. Respondent sought child support, based upon the presumption of paternity contained in Cal. Fam. Code § 7540. The trial court rejected appellant's argument that the statutory exception based upon sterility should be broadly construed to include infertility. Affirming that decision on appeal, the court stated that because a broad interpretation of the sterility exception undermined the intent of the statute, it defined sterility in the strictest sense, that is, that the presumed father could not produce live sperm count. The court held that while application of the presumption might implicate due process concerns where no parent-child relationship had been established, application of the presumption to appellant's case did not deprive him of his right to due process, because appellant did not pursue such a claim in the court below, and had therefore waived the issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment ordering appellant former husband to pay child support, holding that appellant's infertility did not fall within the sterility exception to the conclusive presumption of paternity.

 

681 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Cal. Teachers Ass'n v. Governing Bd.


No. D020372.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


45 Cal. App. 4th 1383; 53 Cal. Rptr. 2d 474; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 504; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3885; 96 Daily Journal DAR 6267



May 30, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County. Super. Ct. No. 669855. Hon. Barbara T. Gamer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the trial court's order granting SDTA's writ is modified to provide district's policy may be enforced against employees engaged in instructional activities. As modified the order is affirmed. Each party to bear its own costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district sought review of an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which granted a writ of petition filed by respondent teachers' union in response to a policy adopted by appellant which prevented its employees from wearing political buttons at work sites during work hours. Respondent's petition also sought declaratory relief, attorney fees, and punitive damages.


OVERVIEW: In response to appellant school district's adoption of a policy which prevented its employees from wearing political buttons at work sites during work hours, respondent teachers' union filed a petition for a writ of mandate as well as for declaratory judgment, attorney fees, and punitive damages. The trial court granted the writ and held that the policy violated U.S. Const. amend. I. The court observed that Cal. Educ. Code § 7055 gave school districts the power to restrict political speech during work hours, but that such power was not unfettered. It also noted that schools had the power to dissociate themselves from political controversy. In striking a balance between appellant's right to dissociate from political activity and the constitutional rights of school employees to express their political viewpoints to one another, the court affirmed the grant of the writ but modified it and held that appellant's ban could be enforced in instructional settings but not in noninstructional settings.


OUTCOME: The court found that appellant school district had the power to prevent its employees from wearing political buttons in instructional settings such as classrooms, however, it held that no such power existed when the employees were not engaged in instructional activities. Thus, the court affirmed the trial court's grant of respondent teachers' union's writ petition but modified it to allow the policy to be enforced in instructional settings.

 

682 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Loyola Marymount Univ. v. L.A. Unified Sch. Dist.


No. B087502.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


45 Cal. App. 4th 1256; 53 Cal. Rptr. 2d 424; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 485; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3831; 96 Daily Journal DAR 6166



May 29, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 28, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 4986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BS027258. Hon. Diane Wayne, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Costs on appeal are awarded to the LAUSD.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted a writ of mandate, and entered judgment, in favor of respondent university. The judgment ordered appellant to refund school development fees to respondent.


OVERVIEW: Respondent university applied for a permit to construct a new business school. Appellant school district would not issue a permit unless respondent paid school development fees, pursuant to Cal. Gov't. Code § 53080. Respondent paid the fees under protest and then filed a petition for writ of mandamus. The trial court granted the petition. On appeal the court reversed, holding first that in enacting § 53080, the legislature intended to include virtually all construction except that exempted by Cal. Gov't. Code § 65995 et seq, which applied only to purely religious and state entities. The court found that respondent and its business school were not purely religious in their activities and that respondent conducted commerce by exchanging money for tuition. The court also held that a statement that enrollment would be unaffected was inadequate to overcome the application of the fees. The court further held that the fees were not taxes so that respondent could not take advantage of tax exemptions under § 65995 or Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 3. Finally the court held that it did not need to apply the essential nexus test for takings cases as it did not apply to development fees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the school development fee refund to respondent university because the authorizing statute applied to all but purely religious and state entities, which respondent was not, the fees were not taxes so that respondent could not take advantage of statutory and constitutional tax exemptions, and development fees did not invoke full takings clause scrutiny.

 

683 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Aguilar v. Avis Rent-a-Car Sys.


No. A069353


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


50 Cal. App. 4th 28; 53 Cal. Rptr. 2d 599; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 471; 70 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1477; 71 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P44,841; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3619; 96 Daily Journal DAR 5899



May 21, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted September 4, 1996 (S054561), Reported at 1996 Cal. LEXIS 5077.
[Reprinted as modified to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 45 Cal. App. 4th 933.)]
Supreme Court of California disposition reported at: 21 Cal. 4th 121.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] San Francisco Superior Court. Super. Ct. No. 948597. Hon. Carlos T. Bea, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, employer and manager, sought review of the injunction portion of a judgment of the San Francisco Superior Court (California), which issued an injunction prohibiting appellant manager from using racial epithets in the workplace in violation of the Fair Employment and Housing Act in an action brought by appellee employees based on harassment that created an abusive work environment under Cal. Gov't Code § 12940(h)(1).


OVERVIEW: Appellee employees filed suit against appellants, employer and manager, for abusive work environment in violation of Cal. Gov't Code § 12940(h)(1), based on derogatory racial epithets directed towards them by appellant manager. After the trial court awarded verdicts to certain appellees, it awarded an injunction ordering appellant manager to refrain from use of racial comments. Appellants sought review of the injunctive portion of the judgment, claiming that the injunction violated the Fair Employment and Housing Act (FEHA), Cal. Gov't Code § 12900 et seq., the U.S. Const. amend. I and Cal. Const. art. I, § 2(a). The court reversed the decision, holding that the injunction reached beyond the scope of FEHA, but ruled that the injunction did not violate the constitutional proscription against prior restraints because it targeted conduct that amounted to employment discrimination, not free expression. There was no direct restraint of free expression, and the injunction was directed at conduct rather than expressive activity. On remand, the trial court was directed to modify and narrow the injunction's scope and describe the prohibited conduct with further specificity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the injunctive portion of the judgment against appellants, employer and manager, that prohibited appellant manager from directing racial epithets toward appellee employees because it violated the Fair Employment and Housing Act by reaching beyond the workplace. The court held that the injunction was constitutional because it targeted secondary effect speech and did not restrain free expression.

People v. Bell


No. D019480.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


45 Cal. App. 4th 1030; 53 Cal. Rptr. 2d 156; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 475; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3660; 96 Daily Journal DAR 5887



May 21, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 28, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 5029.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from judgments of the Superior Court of San Diego County. Super. Ct. No. CR136016. Hon. Lillian Y. Lim, Judge.



DISPOSITION: By operation of law Robert and Cathleen are deemed convicted of one count of rent skimming based on the findings in counts 7, 9, 10, 11 and 12. Any abstract of judgment as to either appellant shall reflect conviction for a single count of rent skimming. All of Robert and Cathleen's other convictions for rent skimming are reversed. Robert's convictions in counts 13 through 21 are reversed and his case is remanded for further proceedings. In all other respects the judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, husband and wife, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted them of several counts of rent skimming in violation of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 890-894.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, husband and wife, were convicted of several counts of rent skimming under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 890-894, forgery, and filing a false instrument, and challenged the convictions, alleging that the rent skimming statute was unconstitutional, the statute of limitations had run, the search warrants were defective, and the jury was misinstructed. The court affirmed the judgment for all counts, but reversed for all but one count of rent skimming as to both appellants, because the acts were barred because they did not occur within three years of commencement of prosecution. The court held that the rent skimming statute was constitutional, because it criminalized the willful diversion of specific funds actually in the possession of the defendants and did not violate the constitutional prohibition against imprisonment for debt. The court ruled that the jury was given proper instructions regarding rent skimming, because the trial court clearly stated that the diversion of rental revenue must have been willful. In addition, the court determined that the search warrants were valid, because they were reasonably relied upon by the officers and were not lacking in probable cause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions of appellants, husband and wife, in part and reversed in part. The court held that the rent skimming statute was constitutional and that the search warrants did not lack sufficient probable cause. Nevertheless, appellants' convictions were reversed for all but one count of rent skimming because the statute of limitations had run before discovery of all of the acts.

California Kiwifruit Com. v. Moss


No. C018368


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


50 Cal. App. 4th 1; 53 Cal. Rptr. 2d 138; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 453; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3569; 96 Daily Journal DAR 5783



May 20, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified on Denial of Rehearing June 17, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 568. Review Granted August 14, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 4463.
[Reprinted as modified to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 45 Cal.App.4th 769.)] Opinion filed March 26, 1998 not for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Super. Ct. No. CV530170. Hon. Ronald W. Tochterman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant farmer challenged the jurisdiction of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted respondent California Kiwifruit Commission assessments owed by appellant for the 1988-1992 marketing seasons.


OVERVIEW: In 1979, the California Legislature established respondent California Kiwifruit Commission to deal with advertising, promotion, marketing research, and production research under Cal. Food & Agric. Code § 68005. Respondent was funded through an assessment upon kiwifruit handlers, pursuant to Cal. Food & Agric. Code §§ 68081(n), 68106. When respondent filed an action against appellant farmer for assessments due for the 1988-1992 marketing seasons, appellant defended on the ground the assessments were an unconstitutional infringement of his U.S. Const. amend. I freedom of speech and association rights. The trial court held in favor of respondent. On appeal, the court applied a strict scrutiny analysis to the assessment because such a standard of review was usually applied in cases involving associational rights. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, holding that the compelled contributions were not narrowly tailored to achieve the goal of promoting kiwifruit, and thus the assessments violated appellant's U.S. Const. amend. I rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court in favor of respondent kiwifruit commission, holding that the compelled contributions were not narrowly tailored to achieve the goal of promoting kiwifruit, and thus the assessments violated appellant farmer's First Amendment rights.

People v. O'Roark


No. B093085


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO

May 15, 1996, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted August 14, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 4461.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 45 Cal. App. 4th 546.)] (See 63 Cal. App. 4th 872).
Transferred January 28, 1998 to Ct. App., 2d Dist., Div. 2, with directions to vacate its decision and reconsider in light of People v. Superior Court (Romero) (1996) 13 Cal. 4th 497.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. SA019408. Hon. Leslie W. Light, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his sentence under the three strikes law, Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)-(i), entered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of three counts of second-degree robbery, using a firearm in the commission of each robbery. The trial court found that defendant had three prior serious felony convictions within the meaning of the three strikes law, and that he had served three prior prison terms.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction and sentence under the three strikes law, Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)-(i), for three counts of second-degree robbery, with findings that he used a firearm in the commission of each robbery. The jury also found that defendant had three prior serious or violent felony convictions, that he had three prior serious felony convictions, and that he served three prior prison terms, all within the meaning of the three strikes law. The court affirmed his conviction and sentence, and found that the three strikes law did not subscribe defendant's punishment since each prior felony he committed was classified as a serious felony on the relevant date for the three strikes law. The court further concluded that defendant's sentence did not violate the prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment pursuant to Cal. Const. art. I, § 17 because it was not grossly disproportionate to defendant's current offense when viewed in light of his recidivist history.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence, found that defendant's prior felonies qualified under the three strikes law, and that defendant's sentence was not cruel or unusual punishment because it was not grossly disproportionate to the current offense when viewed in light of his recidivist history.

Conway v. Pasadena Humane Society


No. B088117.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


45 Cal. App. 4th 163; 52 Cal. Rptr. 2d 777; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 421; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3304; 96 Daily Journal DAR 5357



May 8, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. GC010699. Hon. Coleman A. Swart, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Nicholas and Virginia Conway are entitled to costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homeowners appealed the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which awarded summary judgment to defendants, humane society, city, and animal control officer, in plaintiffs' 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 action on the grounds that defendant animal control officer's warrantless entry into plaintiffs' home to seize a dog was not a violation of U. S. Const. amend. IV.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff homeowners filed suit under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, claiming that defendants, humane society, city, and animal control officer, violated their rights under U. S. Const. amend. IV. Defendant animal control officer observed plaintiffs' dog roaming free in violation of the city's leash law. Defendant animal control officer later entered plaintiffs' residence while they were not home, without a warrant, and seized the dog. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of defendants on the ground that there was no constitutional violation. On appeal, the court reversed and held that the warrantless entry was unlawful. The court held that there were no exigent circumstances requiring entry because the dog did not present a danger, the infraction of the leash law was a minor violation, and there was time to procure a warrant. The court found that Pasadena Mun. Code § 6.08.110, which authorizes animal control officers to enter premises where an animal running at large is kept and to seize it, did not dispense with the requirement of a warrant, and that defendant animal control officer was not entitled to qualified immunity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the award of summary judgment to defendants, humane society, city, and animal control officer, in plaintiff homeowners' civil rights action, because defendant animal control officer's warrantless entry into plaintiffs' home to seize their dog was unlawful under the Fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution because no exigent circumstances existed.

Nast v. State Bd. of Equalization


No. C020897.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


46 Cal. App. 4th 343; 53 Cal. Rptr. 2d 592; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 541; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4127; 96 Daily Journal DAR 6623



May 8, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published June 7, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Super. Ct. No. CV380060. Hon. Ronald W. Tochterman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer sought review of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) dismissal of his petition and sustainment of defendant State Board of Equalization's demurrer to his writ of mandate, by which plaintiff sought to compel defendant to order the Franchise Tax Board to refund taxes assessed against him.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer filed a petition for writ of mandate to compel defendant State Board of Equalization (SBE) to order the Franchise Tax Board (FTB) to issue a refund of taxes he alleged were improperly assessed. SBE demurred and the trial court sustained the demurrer and entered a judgment of dismissal. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of dismissal because Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 19382 and Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 32 provided the remedy of an action for refund, which disqualified plaintiff from seeking a writ of mandate since there was an adequate remedy at law. The court also dismissed plaintiff's contention that, because the limitations period to commence a refund action under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 19384 had expired, he had no adequate remedy at law, and held that plaintiff's inability to avail himself of the remedy, because of statute of limitations problems, did not render the remedy inadequate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment which dismissed plaintiff taxpayer's petition and sustained defendant State Board of Equalization's demurrer to his writ of mandate, because the law prohibited a writ of mandate for assessed taxes, and plaintiff had an adequate remedy at law since he could file an action to recover the tax paid.

People v. Allen


No. B090990


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


49 Cal. App. 4th 247; 52 Cal. Rptr. 2d 749; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 405; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3141; 96 Daily Journal DAR 5146



May 1, 1996, Filed


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted July 31, 1996 (S054125), Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 4226.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 44 Cal. App. 4th 1707.)]
Trans. April 30, 1997 to Ct. App., 2d Dist., Div. 4, with directions to vacate its opn. and reconsider in light of Garcia v. Superior Court (1997) 14 Cal. 4th 953, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 2318.
Opn. filed July 25, 1997 not for publication. Review Granted October 29, 1997 (S054125).


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judge of the Los Angeles Superior Court. Super. Ct. No. YA020812. Hon. Donald F. Pitts, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Los Angeles Superior Court (California), which denied defendant an evidentiary hearing on his motion to collaterally attack a prior conviction alleged for purposes of sentence enhancement, ruled that defendant's prior conviction was true, and issued defendant an enhanced sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed a motion to collaterally attack a prior conviction alleged for purposes of sentencing enhancement on the grounds that his counsel had failed to adequately advise him of his constitutional rights, and therefore the guilty plea he entered was obtained in violation of his constitutional rights. The trial court denied defendant's motion without affording him an evidentiary hearing, relying on a United State's Supreme Court opinion which rejected a defendant's collateral attack of a state court prior conviction where the defendant had been represented by counsel. The trial court issued defendant an enhanced sentence and defendant appealed. The court reversed, holding that a state supreme court case which declined to rule that collateral attacks were only permitted in cases where a denial of counsel was alleged had not been overruled or undermined by later federal supreme court decisions. The court concluded that the accepted principles of stare decisis commanded the trial courts and courts of appeal to follow the state supreme court holding. Thus, defendant's representation by counsel did not preclude him from collaterally attacking a prior conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment that denied defendant an evidentiary hearing on his motion to collaterally attack a prior conviction, and remanded the matter, because the accepted principles of stare decisis commanded the trial court to follow the state supreme court holdings, not the federal supreme court holdings, which did not preclude collateral attacks of prior convictions where the defendant had been represented by counsel.

 

690 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Sinclair Paint Co. v. State Bd. of Equalization


No. C021559


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


49 Cal. App. 4th 127; 52 Cal. Rptr. 2d 572; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 396; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3036; 96 Daily Journal DAR 4990



April 30, 1996, Filed


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted July 31, 1995 (S054115), Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 4225.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 44 Cal. App. 4th 1434.)] (See 15 Cal. 4th 866)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of the County of Sacramento. Super. Ct. No. CV541310. Hon. Joe S. Gray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state agencies, sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of the County of Sacramento (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent paint company, in an action brought by respondent for a refund of its fees paid pursuant to the Childhood Lead Poisoning Prevention Act, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 105275 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Respondent paint company was assessed fees in accordance with the Childhood Lead Poisoning Prevention Act (Act), Cal. Health & Safety Code § 105275 et seq. Subsequently, the trial court granted summary judgment in favor of respondent's claim for a refund of its fee payment pursuant to Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 3. Thereafter, appellant state agencies sought review. On review, the court affirmed the judgment. The court found that the fees were taxes and thus violated the state constitution. The court noted that there was nothing on the face of the Act to show that the fees collected were used to regulate respondent and that the Act did not require respondent to comply with any other conditions. The court ruled that where a payment was exacted solely for revenue purposes and the payment gave the right to carry on the business without any further conditions, it was a tax. The court also noted that even if the Act imposed a tax, that tax would be invalid because the legislature did not pass the Act by a two-thirds majority. The court also ruled that summary judgment was appropriate because the issue as to whether the payment constituted a tax was a question of law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed respondent paint company's summary judgment against appellant state agencies, because the fees assessed against respondent where unconstitutional taxes. The court found that the statute in question did not show that the fees were used to regulate respondent and that respondent was not required to comply with any conditions under the statute.

 

691 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. McDaniel


No. A068870


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


44 Cal. App. 4th 1590; 52 Cal. Rptr. 2d 595; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 398; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3086; 96 Daily Journal DAR 5018



April 30, 1996, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied August 14, 1996 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 4486.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court County of San Francisco. Super. Ct. No. 157415. Hon. Ina Levin Gyemant, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court, County of San Francisco, California that convicted him for possessing marijuana for sale and for selling marijuana.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of three counts of possessing marijuana for sale on three different dates. He was also convicted of selling marijuana. On appeal, the court affirmed the convictions. Consecutive sentences were proper because the acts were sufficiently different from each other in intent and objective. Appellant's crimes did not occur on the same day but were spaced out over a period of 34 days. In addition, appellant was not in possession of the same bags of marijuana on different days. Moreover, one possession was part and parcel of an aborted sale, while another possession involved no sale component. The trial court properly doubled the subordinate terms because Cal. Penal Code § 667(c)(6) provides for sentencing a defendant consecutively on each count pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 667(e), which provides for doubling, so the determinate term for each count should be doubled. Finally, by the plain language of Cal. Penal Code § 667(f)(2), a prosecutor may only move to dismiss or strike a prior felony conviction "allegation." Once the prior felony conviction was proven, the prosecutor lost this power.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for possessing marijuana for sale and for selling marijuana. Consecutive sentences were proper because the offenses did not arise from the same set of operative facts. The subordinate terms were properly doubled because the determinate term on each count could be doubled. The prosecutor did not have the power to strike appellant's prior felony conviction once it was proven.

 

692 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Ruiz


No. F022708.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


44 Cal. App. 4th 1653; 52 Cal. Rptr. 2d 561; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 399; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3104; 96 Daily Journal DAR 5024



April 30, 1996, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of the Statement of Facts, parts I., II.A., II.B., II.D., II.F., II.G., and IIIB.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 511983-9, Gary S. Austin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified by striking the trial court's reservation of jurisdiction to award victim restitution. The trial court shall prepare an amended abstract of judgment and forward it to the appropriate authorities. As so modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), in which he was convicted of second-degree murder involving the use of a deadly weapon and sentenced to 37 years' imprisonment. Appellant challenged the legislative version of the Three Strikes Laws as the infliction of cruel and unusual punishment and the imposition of sentencing enhancements under Cal. Penal Code §§ 667(a), 667.5(b).


OVERVIEW: On review, the court rejected appellant's contention that the legislative version of the Three Strikes Law resulted in the infliction of cruel and unusual punishment upon appellant as a "second striker," in violation of the guarantees under U.S. Const. amend. VIII. The court noted that although Cal. Penal Code § 190 established the minimum term of imprisonment, the statute did not mandate that a murderer had to be sentenced under its provision to the exclusion of any other sentencing scheme. The court further found that appellant had failed to show that the disparity of punishments in California and other jurisdictions, noting that California's Three Strikes scheme was consistent with the nationwide pattern of substantially increasing sentences for habitual offenders. Although the court did direct the modification of the judgment to strike the reservation of jurisdiction to award victim restitution, the court affirmed as modified. The court concluded that the imposition of both a Cal. Penal Code § 667(a) and a Cal. Penal Code § 667.5(b) sentencing enhancement were appropriate because the enhancements were based on different offenses.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified to strike the reservation of jurisdiction to award victim restitution, but affirmed in all other respects. The legislative version of the Three Strikes Law did not result in cruel and unusual punishment where appellant failed to show a sentencing disparity among the jurisdictions, and the imposition of sentencing enhancements under two penal statutes was proper where the enhancements were for separate prior offenses.

 

693 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Moore


No. A068390.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


44 Cal. App. 4th 1323; 52 Cal. Rptr. 2d 256; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 383; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2954; 96 Daily Journal DAR 4851



April 25, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Marin County, No. SC060647A, Lynn Duryee, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Municipal Court for the Marin Judicial District sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction under Cal. Penal Code § 273.5, which outlawed the inflicting of corporal punishment on a cohabitant or spouse. After the appeal was filed, the Court of Appeal, First Appellate District (California) requested briefing on the substantive issue of simultaneous cohabitation, which the jurors originally raised in a written question to the trial court.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction of inflicting corporal punishment on a cohabitant or spouse in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 273.5. The court of appeal affirmed. Defendant's simultaneous cohabitation with two or more people at different locations was not dispositive because he maintained a substantial ongoing relationship with the victim and lived with her for significant periods. There was sufficient evidence to support the jury's finding that defendant was cohabiting with the victim at the time of the subject incidents, whether or not he was simultaneously cohabiting with one or more other women. Although the trial court erred in leaving to the jury the essentially legal question of whether there could in fact be simultaneous cohabitation, there was no possible prejudice in this case. The trial court provided a proper definition of cohabitation. The jury's conclusion that simultaneous cohabitation could exist could have stood on the correct legal conclusion that a person could simultaneously cohabit with two or more individuals at different locations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction. There was sufficient evidence to support the jury's finding that defendant was cohabiting with the victim at the time of the subject incidents of corporal punishment, whether or not he was simultaneously cohabiting with one or more other women. The trial court provided a proper definition of cohabitation.

 

694 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Estate of Hilton


No. B084575.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


44 Cal. App. 4th 890; 52 Cal. Rptr. 2d 491; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 378; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2835; 96 Daily Journal DAR 4720



April 23, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. P644729, Lester E. Olson, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The order of the trial court is affirmed. Each party is to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney sought review of an order entered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his request for attorney fees for extraordinary services rendered to the personal representatives of an estate, pursuant to Cal. Prob. Code § 10811. Respondent beneficiary challenged the same order, which denied its request that petitioner repay the estate a portion of the statutory attorney fees he was awarded.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney had been awarded statutory and extraordinary attorney fees for estate representation. Petitioner filed a Cal. Prob. Code § 10811 request for an additional amount for extraordinary services performed during the 12-year period since his previous award, and after the statutory fee rate was lowered. Respondent beneficiary objected, and claimed that petitioner was previously overpaid for statutory fees because the rate was since lowered, and requested that he repay a portion of the statutory attorney fees he had been awarded. The court affirmed the denial of the fee request and the repayment request. The court found that petitioner's prior fee awards, which were not appealed, were vested, legally enforceable rights that could not be modified. The court held that the probate court had no power to order repayment of sums awarded to petitioner because allowances of statutory attorney fees were not subject to downward modification once the time to appeal expired. The court also held that the probate court had discretion to deny extraordinary fees where statutory fees reasonably compensated for statutory and uncompensated extraordinary services.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying petitioner attorney's request for extraordinary fees and respondent beneficiary's request that petitioner repay alleged fee overpayments. The court held that the probate court had no power to order repayment because allowances of statutory fees could not be modified downward, and that it had discretion to deny extraordinary fees where statutory fees compensate for statutory and extraordinary services.

People v. Renko


No. D023059


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


48 Cal. App. 4th 1417; 52 Cal. Rptr. 2d 45; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 334; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2693; 96 Daily Journal DAR 4404



April 15, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted July 10, 1996 (S053739), Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 3828.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 44 Cal. App. 4th 620.)]
Opinion Filed January 14, 1999 not for Publication.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County. Super. Ct. No. SCN003157. Hon. Lisa Guy-Schall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part; reversed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which entered a conviction on the charge of residential burglary, Cal. Penal Code § 459, § 460, receiving stolen property, Cal. Penal Code § 496(a), and resisting arrest, Cal. Penal Code § 148. Defendant contended that it was error for the trial court to sentence him under the three strikes law, Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)(i), on the basis of prior juvenile convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of residential burglary, Cal. Penal Code § 459, § 460, receiving stolen property, Cal. Penal Code § 496(a), and resisting arrest, Cal. Penal Code § 148. Defendant was sentenced under the three strikes law, Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)(i), on the basis of prior juvenile convictions. Defendant challenged his sentence. The court held that the three strikes law included an express reference to a finding of fitness that the Legislature intended to be made before a juvenile offense could be treated a strike within the meaning of the three strikes law. In order for a juvenile offense to be included as a strike, the court had to make a finding on the basis of five criteria enumerated in Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707, and determine the minor's amenability and fitness to being dealt with under juvenile court law. The court affirmed defendant's conviction, but reversed the sentence imposed on him because the required procedures were not followed. The court remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions but concluded that the trial court erred by using defendant's prior juvenile adjudications as prior serious or violent felony convictions in sentencing him under the three strikes law. The court remanded for resentencing and instructed the trial court to consider the applicability of Cal. Penal Code § 667(a)(1).

Conrad v. Bank of America


No. C016482.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


45 Cal. App. 4th 133; 53 Cal. Rptr. 2d 336; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 323; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2546; 96 Daily Journal DAR 4178



April 9, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 5, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 4426.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of El Dorado County. Super. Ct. No. 51426. Hon. Lloyd B. Hamilton, Judge. * 


*  Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.

Opinion Previously Reported at: 44 Cal. App. 4th 317, 52 Cal. Rptr. 2d 142. Opinion Reprinted to Include the Opinion on Denial of Rehearing on May 7, 1997.



DISPOSITION: For these reasons we find nothing in the Ryan Operations decision that casts doubt on the reasoning or conclusion of our decision in this case. Nothing else that plaintiffs have raised in their petition for rehearing requires comment. The petition for rehearing is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants claimants sought review of a judgment notwithstanding the verdict from the Superior Court of El Dorado County (California) after a jury had ruled against appellees banks in a fraud action for appellants' failure to list or otherwise identify their claims during their bankruptcy proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Appellants claimants filed a lawsuit against appellee bank based on an alleged false promise to enter into a loan contract with them. The jury found in favor of appellants in the fraud action; however, the trial court granted appellees banks' motion for judgment notwithstanding the verdict based upon appellants' failure to list or otherwise identify their claims during their bankruptcy proceedings. On appeal, the court held that appellants' claim for fraud was barred since they failed to list or otherwise identify the claim in their bankruptcy proceedings up to and through the time the bankruptcy court issued orders confirming their reorganization plans. The court found that the evidence was insufficient to support a cause of action for fraud. Appellants failed to show damage specifically attributable to the short-lived promise of a loan and accordingly failed to establish a cause of action for fraud based upon that promise. Therefore, the judgment of the trial court was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant to appellees banks of a judgment notwithstanding the verdict. Appellants claimants' claim for fraud was barred since they failed to list or otherwise identify the claim in their bankruptcy proceedings up to and through the time the bankruptcy court issued orders confirming their reorganization plans.

Suzuki v. City of Los Angeles


No. B091628.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


44 Cal. App. 4th 263; 51 Cal. Rptr. 2d 880; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 306; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2507; 96 Daily Journal DAR 3997



April 8, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BS027313, Judith C. Chirlin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the order granting relief from stay of the trial court judgment is vacated. Each side to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found for respondent owners of a liquor store, prohibited appellant from imposing conditions to abate nuisance activities at respondents' business, and granted respondents relief from a stay of judgment pending review of matter.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city challenged a judgment prohibiting it from imposing conditions to abate nuisance activities at respondent liquor store owners' business, and the granting of relief from a stay of judgment pending review. The issue was whether the Alcoholic Beverage Control Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 23790, prevented appellant from using its administrative procedures to abate nuisances by imposing conditions on the continued operation of the liquor store which had been in continuous operation prior to the enactment of the city's nuisance abatement ordinance. The court held that the statute did not exempt a licensee, who allowed his business to be maintained as a nuisance, from appellant's administrative procedures to abate what had been found, after proper notice and hearing, to constitute a nuisance. Further, respondents were not immune from appellant's administrative procedures to abate nuisance activity at their liquor store despite their status as a licensed but nonconforming use. The judgment was therefore reversed, and the order granting relief from the stay of the trial court's judgment was vacated.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the order granting relief from stay of judgment was vacated. Appellant city could impose conditions to abate nuisance activities at respondent liquor store owners' place of business, because the Alcoholic Beverage Control Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 23790, did not prevent appellant from imposing conditions on continued operation of the liquor store.
