Pipe Trades Dist. Council No. 51 v. Aubry


No. A068765.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


41 Cal. App. 4th 1457; 49 Cal. Rptr. 2d 208; 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 60; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 554; 96 Daily Journal DAR 861; 133 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P58,241



January 4, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published January 24, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 961722, Stuart R. Pollak, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders denying the petition for a writ of mandate are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff trade union and others appealed the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) that denied their petition for a writ of mandate to compel defendant Department of Industrial Relations to publish a jurisdictional agreement that involved another union.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff trade union and others sought to compel defendant Department of Industrial Relations to undo its administrative decision not to publish as a regulation a jurisdictional agreement with another union that concerned prevailing wage rates. The trial court found for defendant. The reviewing court affirmed the denial of plaintiffs' petition for a writ of mandate. The court found that a writ of mandamus did not lie to control exercise of defendant's discretion, especially given the fact that it was engaged in rulemaking. Defendant's decision was subject only to arbitrary and capricious review, and if it had adopted the rates established by plaintiff it might have violated the prohibition against delegating administrative rulemaking authority to private parties. The agreement was not enforceable as a collective bargaining agreement because employers and contractors had not signed it. Moreover, the jurisdictional agreement involved matters with which defendant did not have to concern itself, and was of limited scope. Defendant could not be compelled to exceed his statutory authority by equitable considerations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court that denied plaintiffs' petition for a writ of mandate because such writs were not available to control the exercise of an agency's discretion and the agreement was unenforceable because it was not signed by employers. The agreement concerned matters that did not concern defendant, and it could not be compelled to exceed his statutory authority because of a prior agreement with plaintiff.

 

729 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Domar Electric, Inc. v. City of Los Angeles


No. B073387.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


41 Cal. App. 4th 810; 48 Cal. Rptr. 2d 822; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1275; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 99; 96 Daily Journal DAR 34



December 28, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified January 25, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BS020805, Robert H. O'Brien, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff contractor appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles (California) in a public contract case against defendant city that was on remand.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff contractor submitted the lowest monetary bid on a public works project for defendant city. However, defendant awarded the contract to intervener bidder because it found that plaintiff had not complied with a subcontractor outreach program by making good faith efforts to use minority and women-owned business enterprises. Plaintiff claimed that the program violated defendant's charter, Cal. Pub. Cont. Code § 2000, and equal protection. The state supreme court found that the program was not inconsistent with the charter and remanded for consideration of the remaining claims. Section 2000 was intended to occupy the entire field with respect to programs designed to encourage use of minority- and women-owned business enterprises, but the program did not violate the statute. Section 2000(a)(1), which allowed contractors to achieve a set percentage of minority/women owned subcontractor participation, had been found to violate equal protection unless there was a showing of past discrimination in the industry. Therefore, the program could require good faith efforts only and was constitutional. Accordingly, the court affirmed the trial court judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment for defendant city because defendant's outreach program was consistent with that part of the statute governing subcontracting that did not violate equal protection. Plaintiff contractor could not comply with state law with a set level of women and minority participation because there had been no showing of past discrimination in the industry.

People v. Muhamed


No. G016604.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


50 Cal. App. 4th 403; 48 Cal. Rptr. 2d 687; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1249; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9827; 95 Daily Journal DAR 16984



December 21, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted March 21, 1996 (S051310), Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1650.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 45 Cal. App. 4th 1526.)]
Review Dismissed April 1, 1998 and cause remanded to Ct. App., 4th Dist., Div. 3 (rule 29.4(c), Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2097.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-99714, Kazuharu Makino, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which found true a gang enhancement under Cal. Penal Code § 186.22 to his conviction for first degree murder and assault with a firearm under Cal. Penal Code §§ 187(a) and 189.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and assault with a firearm under Cal. Penal Code §§ 187(a) and 189 after he and several fellow gang members attacked and killed a boy on his way home from school. The trial court found true the allegations that he committed the offenses for the benefit of a street gang under Cal. Penal Code § 186.22. Defendant appealed the gang enhancement, arguing that the evidence was insufficient to support the fact that his gang existed at the time an earlier incident occurred; thus, he argued, there was no pattern shown. The reviewing court affirmed, finding that it was irrelevant whether or not the earlier incident was gang activity because, under § 186.22, a pattern of criminal gang activity was properly shown exclusively from the charged offenses. The court found that because another gang member was charged for a separate offense for the same incident, the requirements of Cal. Penal Code § 186.22 (e) were met. The court found that such interpretation did not violate defendant's due process or freedom of association rights. Nor did the statute violate Cal. Penal Code § 654's prohibition against punishing defendants twice for the same crime.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's finding of a gang enhancement, holding that the requisite pattern of gang activity was properly shown exclusively from the currently charged offenses and that such application did not violate defendant's freedom of association or due process rights.

People v. Markson

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted March 21, 1996 (S051506), Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1653.

DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to stay the sentence on count 1; in all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of mayhem, assault with a deadly weapon, and the lesser-included offense of carjacking. Appellant raised several issues involving the sentencing scheme set forth in the three strikes law, Cal. Penal Code, §667(b)-(i).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted by a jury of mayhem, assault with a deadly weapon, and the lesser-included offense of attempted carjacking. Appellant was sentenced to 25 years to life, pursuant to the three strikes law, for the mayhem conviction, to a consecutive term of 25 years to life plus three years for the great bodily injury enhancement for the attempted carjacking, and to 25 years to life plus three years for the assault conviction, sentence to be stayed pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 654. Appellant was sentenced to five years for each prior conviction pursuant to Cal. Penal Code 667(a)(1), to run consecutive to the sentence for mayhem and carjacking convictions. Appellant sought review, and the court modified the judgment to stay the sentence on the mayhem conviction and otherwise affirmed the judgment of conviction. The court held that the trial court erred in sentencing appellant consecutively for mayhem and attempted carjacking because both crimes were committed on the same occasion and arose from the same set of operative facts.


OUTCOME: The court held that the trial court erred in sentencing appellant consecutively for mayhem and attempted carjacking because both crimes were committed on the same occasion and arose from the same set of operative facts. The court modified the judgment to stay the sentence on the mayhem conviction and otherwise affirmed the judgment of conviction.

Association of Community Organizations for Reform Now v. Department of Industrial Relations


No. A069744.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


41 Cal. App. 4th 298; 48 Cal. Rptr. 2d 486; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1255; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9792; 95 Daily Journal DAR 17017; 131 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P58,049



December 21, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 962283, William J. Cahill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant advocacy organization sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) sustaining a demurrer filed by respondent, Department of Industrial Relations, Division of Labor Standards Enforcement, and entering judgment for respondent. Appellant had requested a declaration that California's minimum wage laws were unconstitutional as applied.


OVERVIEW: Appellant advocacy organization, an Arkansas corporation, employed workers in California to recruit members for local community organizations affiliated with the parent organization in order to promote its social agenda, circulate petitions, and solicit financial contributions. Appellant paid workers in varying ways that may not have risen to the level of California's minimum wage as specified in Cal. Code Regs. tit. 8, § 11000 (2). Appellant contended that the minimum wage laws, while facially constitutional, were unconstitutional as applied to it because they restricted its ability to engage in political advocacy. In affirming the trial judge, the court ruled that just as with a facial constitutional challenge, any incidental infringement on appellant's freedoms under U.S. Const. amend. I could be justified by compelling state interests, unrelated to the suppression of ideas, that could not be achieved through means significantly less restrictive of associational freedoms.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment entered against appellant advocacy group and held that California's minimum wage laws were not unconstitutional as applied to an organization whose purpose was political advocacy. The compelling state interest in ensuring a minimum wage adequate to maintain a decent standard of living justified any incidental infringement on appellant's First Amendment freedoms.

 

733 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Waisbren v. Peppercorn Productions, Inc.


No. B088448.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


41 Cal. App. 4th 246; 48 Cal. Rptr. 2d 437; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1237; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9734; 95 Daily Journal DAR 16899



December 20, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 14, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1553.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. EC001909, Thomas C. Murphy, Judge. * 


* Retired judge of the Los Angeles Superior Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant personal manager/talent agent challenged an order by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of appellee entertainment company. Appellant had filed the underlying action for breach of a representation agreement between the parties.


OVERVIEW: Appellant personal manager/talent agent sought review of a summary judgment in favor of appellee entertainment company. Appellant had filed the underlying action against appellee seeking damages for appellee's breach of a representation agreement between the parties. The trial court ruled that the parties' agreement was void because appellant had performed the duties of a talent agent by procuring employment for appellee without first obtaining the necessary license. Appellant admitted that he had no such license, but contended that a license was unnecessary because his procurement activities were minimal and merely incidental to his other responsibilities. The court found that appellant was not exempt from the licensing requirement, which applied equally to both full-time and part-time talent agents. Therefore, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of appellee, ruling that the representation agreement between the parties was illegal and unenforceable.


OUTCOME: The judgment in favor of appellee entertainment company was affirmed because appellant personal manger/talent agent was not licensed and therefore had no grounds upon which to enforce a representation agreement that had been breached by appellee.

 

734 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Arellanes v. Civil Serv. Com


No. B079842.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


41 Cal. App. 4th 1208; 49 Cal. Rptr. 2d 73; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1284; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 358; 96 Daily Journal DAR 541



December 18, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published January 16, 1996. Review Denied March 20, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1594.
Review denied by Arellanes v. Los Angeles County Civil Serv., 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1594 (Cal., Mar. 20, 1996)
Related proceeding at California Science Center v. State Personnel Bd., 2013 Cal. App. LEXIS 660 (Cal. App. 2d Dist., July 25, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BS012217, Robert H. O'Brien, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Arellanes to bear costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant officer sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) denying his petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent Los Angeles County Civil Service Commission to vacate its order upholding appellant's discharge from the Los Angeles County Sheriff's Department.


OVERVIEW: Appellant officer was implicated in an extortion scheme after it was alleged that he was providing information from the Los Angeles County Sheriff's Department's computers. As part of the department's internal criminal probe, an extortion defendant met with and spoke to appellant by phone on several occasions to prompt him to divulge department information. As a result of these contacts, appellant's employment was terminated and respondent commission affirmed. Appellant sought review after the lower court denied his petition for writ of mandate to compel respondent to vacate its order. The court affirmed, finding that appellant was not entrapped because the department's investigation did no more than give him an opportunity to act improperly. Appellant violated department rules against associating with criminals and bringing discredit to the department through those associations. He repeatedly had contact with a known criminal whose inquiries were improper. The rules were not vague because they gave appellant sufficient warning of prohibited conduct. Appellant's associations did not trigger U.S. Const. amend. I scrutiny where there was no significant expressive component.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying appellant officer's petition for writ of mandate to compel respondent commission to vacate its order upholding his discharge from the Los Angeles County Sheriff's Department was affirmed. Department rules against associations with criminals and criminal activity were not vague. Appellant's associations did not trigger constitutional scrutiny and the department's probe was not entrapment.

 

735 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Terry H.


No. E015224.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


40 Cal. App. 4th 1675; 47 Cal. Rptr. 2d 791; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1223; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9619; 95 Daily Journal DAR 16637



December 14, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Riverside County, No. J-83565, Patrick Magers, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juvenile sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which found that he came within the provisions of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 after he was found in possession of an imitation controlled substance in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11680. Defendant challenged the application and constitutionality of the statute.


OVERVIEW: Defendant juvenile was found to come within the provisions of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602, and committed to juvenile hall after he was found in possession of an imitation controlled substance, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11680. Defendant was found in possession of blotter paper similar to the papers that typically contained LSD as well as pay or owe sheets that an expert witness testified were similar to the records kept by drug dealers. Defendant admitted to police that he intended to sell the blotter paper to persons who thought it contained LSD. The court held that blotter papers fell within the definition of an imitation controlled substance even thought the Legislature had originally intended a narrower application of the law. The court rejected defendant's challenge to the law on due process grounds for facial vagueness. The court held that the statute provided a reasonable definition of a standard of conduct for those activities that it proscribed. The court affirmed defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for possession of imitation controlled substance and the juvenile hall sentence imposed on him by the trial court. The court held that blotter papers fell within the definition of an imitation controlled substance and found that the law provided a reasonable definition of a proscribed standard of conduct and thus was not void for vagueness.

 

736 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Kinsey


No. B087693.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


40 Cal. App. 4th 1621; 47 Cal. Rptr. 2d 769; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1218; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9568; 95 Daily Journal DAR 16538



December 13, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 14, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1550.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. MA008222, Haig Kehiayan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that convicted him of attempted injury on a cohabitant, battery and assault under Cal Penal Code §§ 664/273.5(a), 242 and 240, and sentenced him as a repeat offender under Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)-(i) to 29 years to life imprisonment. Appellant claimed that attempted injury was not a crime and that his sentence violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 17.


OVERVIEW: When appellant shoved his pregnant, live-in girlfriend, a few friends physically separated them and struggled with appellant to keep him away from his girlfriend until the police arrived. As a result, appellant was convicted of attempted injury on a cohabitant, battery and assault under Cal. Penal Code §§ 664/273.5(a), 242 and 240, was found to have committed three prior felonies pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)-(i), and was sentenced to 29 years to life in prison. On appeal, the court held that § 664/273.5(a) did not require a traumatic injury, that under Cal. Penal Code § 273.5(c) less harm needed to be inflicted on a cohabitant than on others before an offense is committed, and that Cal. Penal Code § 1159 allowed the jury to find appellant guilty of the attempt to commit the offense. The court held that appellant's sentence under § 668(b)-(i) was not based on his current offense, but on his status as a recidivist who was a manifest danger to society and who had to be segregated from society for an extended period of time. Thus, appellant's sentence was not cruel and unusual and did not violate Cal. Const. art. I, § 17, and the court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions for attempted injury on a cohabitant, battery and assault, and the 29 year to life sentence. The court held that appellant's attempt to inflict even minor injuries on a cohabitant was a crime because of the greater legal protections given to cohabitants, and that appellant's sentence as a recidivist as well as for his current offenses was not cruel and unusual, but necessary to protect society.

 

737 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Hamilton


No. B088802.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


40 Cal. App. 4th 1615; 47 Cal. Rptr. 2d 749; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1225; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9570; 95 Daily Journal DAR 16536



December 13, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 14, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1554.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BA098835, Michael A. Tynan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California) that convicted him of petty theft, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 666, and sentenced him to a four-year state prison term.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was found guilty of petty theft, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 666. The trial court found an alleged serious felony "strike" under Cal. Penal Code § 667 to be true. Appellant was sentenced to a four-year prison term. Appellant sought review. The court found that the three strikes legislation was not vague because it provided that if appellant had one prior felony conviction that had been pleaded and proved, the determinate term was twice the term otherwise provided as punishment. Cal. Penal Code § 667(e)(1). Appellant's four-year sentence was affirmed because it was a two-year term for the petty theft conviction, which was doubled. The court found that appellant's right to equal protection was not violated because others similarly situated were treated like appellant. The provisions in Cal. Penal Code § 667(c)(5) that limited custody credits did not deprive appellant of equal protection.


OUTCOME: A judgment of the lower court that convicted appellant of petty theft and sentenced him to imprisonment was affirmed because the three strikes legislation allowed the court to impose twice the term otherwise provided as punishment because appellant had a previous felony conviction.

 

738 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Brantley


No. A067812.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


40 Cal. App. 4th 1538; 48 Cal. Rptr. 2d 313; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1205; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9495; 95 Daily Journal DAR 16481



December 12, 1995, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied March 14, 1996 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Offical Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1540.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 941106-7, Allen L. Norris, Judge. * 


* Retired Judge of the Municipal Court for the Bay Judicial District sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court, Contra Costa County (California), which convicted defendant of receiving stolen property pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 496, and unlawfully driving or taking a vehicle without the owner's consent pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 10851(a). Defendant's term of two years was doubled to four years under the Three Strikes law, Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)-(i).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of receiving stolen property pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 496, and unlawfully driving or taking a vehicle without the owner's consent pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 10851(a). His sentence was doubled under the Three Strikes law, Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)-(i). Defendant argued that because the 20 percent limitation on conduct credits in § 667(b)-(i) did not apply to time spent in presentence custody, it was unconstitutional, and a violation of the guarantee of equal protection because it resulted in an irrationally disparate treatment of offenders depending on how much time they spent in presentence custody. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, holding that defendant's equal protection argument had no merit because different classes of detainees were not similarly situated for equal protection purposes, and were not entitled to the same conduct credit, or the same ratio of conduct credit, for time spent in custody or detention. Pretrial detainees were still presumptively innocent; for strike prison inmates, the rehabilitative element of incarceration was overshadowed by the punishment element, calling for a longer period of incarceration.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court and held that defendant's guarantee of equal protection was not violated by the 20 percent limitation on conduct credits in the Three Strikes law, because different classes of detainees were not similarly situated for equal protection purposes and were not entitled to the same conduct credit, or ratio of conduct credit, for time spent in custody or detention.

 

739 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Kwolek


No. H012939.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


40 Cal. App. 4th 1521; 48 Cal. Rptr. 2d 325; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1204; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9541; 95 Daily Journal DAR 16491



December 12, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 43-04109, Thomas E. Kelly, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Municipal Court for the Santa Cruz Judicial District sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The order imposing a $ 5,000 restitution fine is stricken. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California) which convicted him of attempted voluntary manslaughter and found true enhanced allegations of use of a deadly weapon and infliction of great bodily injury. The trial court sentenced defendant to a prison term, ordered him to pay restitution to the State Board of Control, and imposed but stayed an additional restitution fine.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with attempted murder of his wife. A jury found him guilty of attempted voluntary manslaughter and enhanced allegations of deadly weapon use and infliction of great bodily injury. In addition to imposing a term of years, the trial court ordered defendant to pay restitution to the State Board of Control and imposed an additional restitution fine, which it stayed. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment and struck the additional restitution fine. The court held that the trial properly admitted for impeachment purposes evidence of defendant's prior conviction for possession of marijuana for sale pursuant to Cal. Evid. Code § 352. Restitution to the State Board of Control was proper pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1202.4(f), the court held. Because the 1994 amendment to §1202.4(f) merely directed to whom restitution payments would be made without changing the substantive provisions of the law, the court found that it was not punishment and could be applied retroactively to defendant. Finally, the court held, the trial court erred in imposing the additional restitution fine, which was in direct conflict with the language of former Cal. Gov't Code § 13967(c).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence for attempted voluntary manslaughter against his wife, but, in light of restitution ordered paid to the state board of control, struck the additional restitution fine imposed by the trial court.

 

740 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Howard Jarvis Taxpayers' Assn. v. Fresno Metropolitan Projects Authority


No. F023727.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


40 Cal. App. 4th 1359; 48 Cal. Rptr. 2d 269; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1211; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9525; 95 Daily Journal DAR 16419



December 11, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 20, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1638.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 499234-3, Stephen J. Kane, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Fresno Metropolitan Projects Authority appealed a judgment from the Superior Court, Fresno County, No. 499234-3, California, in a suit brought by plaintiffs, two citizens' groups and individual taxpayers, which declared that a tax levied by the authority was invalid.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, two citizens groups and individual taxpayers, brought an action against the Fresno Metropolitan Projects Authority seeking a declaration that a tax collected and administered by the authority was invalid. The superior court held that the legislature had unlawfully delegated to a private body, the authority, the power to levy the tax, in violation of Cal. Const. art. XI, § 11. On appeal, the court affirmed. Despite voter approval for the tax, it was the authority, not the people, who imposed the tax. The legislature's act was subject to ratification by the people, but this did not mean voters delegated the power to tax to the authority. The legislature also could not delegate to the voters the power to delegate to a private person or body the power to levy taxes. The authority was a private body. Most of its board members were from private organizations, and the legislature could not create an office, determine that the office would be filled by an officer not selected by the public, and then give that office the power to levy a tax. Finally, art. XI, § 11 did not contain an exception for "regional" as opposed to "local" taxes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the declaratory judgment in a suit brought by plaintiffs, two citizens' groups and individual taxpayers, that a tax administered by the Fresno Metropolitan Projects Authority violated the California Constitution. The authority was a private body, and the legislature could not delegate to a private body the power to levy taxes.

 

741 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Royster Construction Co. v. Urban West Communities


No. B083429.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


40 Cal. App. 4th 1158; 47 Cal. Rptr. 2d 684; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1186; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9320; 95 Daily Journal DAR 16178



December 6, 1995, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Part "3. Prejudgment Interest" of the Discussion (and appropriate footnotes) of this opinion is not certified for publication. (See Cal. Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NWC033888, Judith M. Ryan, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons set forth above, the judgment is modified to delete the award of attorney fees to Royster on its mechanic's lien claim. The judgment is modified to award prejudgment interest of 7 percent on the mechanic's lien claim from May 9, 1987, to the date of judgment. On the breach of contract claim, the judgment is modified to award prejudgment interest of 7 percent for breach of contract from May 9, 1987, to the date of judgment and prejudgment interest of 7 percent for lost profits from November 24, 1987, through the date of judgment. The judgment is affirmed in all other respects. Appellants to recover their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant land developers sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which awarded attorney fees against the release bond on the mechanic's lien claim.


OVERVIEW: Appellant land developers entered into a contract with respondent construction company for respondent to act as grading subcontract. Appellant terminated the agreement and respondent filed two mechanic's liens and brought suit for breach of contract and to foreclose the liens. The trial court found in favor of respondent. The court modified the judgment to delete the award of attorney fees against the release bond on respondent's mechanic's lien claim. It held that attorney's fees were not recoverable on a mechanic's lien claim and the action against the release bond surety did not change the cause of action to foreclose the mechanic's lien in any way. The court determined that since the rights of the parties were not changed by the posting of a release bond, and the cause of action to foreclose the mechanic's lien was also unchanged as a result, respondent's remedies remained the same, including the bar against recovery of attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment to delete the award of attorney fees against the release bond on respondent construction company's mechanic's lien claim. It held that damages recoverable on a mechanic's lien claim did not include attorney fees and the posting of a release bond did not change the remedies available to respondent.

 

742 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Berry v. City of Santa Barbara


No. B085586.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SIX


40 Cal. App. 4th 1075; 47 Cal. Rptr. 2d 661; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1181; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9267; 95 Daily Journal DAR 16107



December 5, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing January 3, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 4.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 199647, Patrick L. McMahon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We affirm the trial court's ruling that section 5.66.110 of the Santa Barbara Municipal Code is facially constitutional. We reverse the order requiring "L.A. X . . . Press" to be blinded, and remand for an express ruling on whether its front page is harmful to minors. Each party shall bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant newsstand owner challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which found an ordinance that prohibited display of adult publications in newsracks without blinder racks to be constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant newsstand owner distributed an adult publication from locked, sidewalk newsracks. When respondent city passed an ordinance prohibiting public display of material harmful to minors unless binder racks were placed in front of the material, appellant filed suit challenging its constitutionality. The trial court granted respondent's motion for judgment on the pleadings. The court held that the statute was constitutional because it was narrowly tailored to eliminate the harmful matter on the lower two-thirds of the front page of the publication. However, the blinders could only be required if the front page of the publication contained the harmful matter. The court held that to the extent the trial court's ruling was predicated on a finding that harmful matter inside a publication provided a basis for blinding the cover, it was erroneous, and remanded the issue for a determination of whether the front page was harmful to minors. It also held that the ordinance did not violate the equal protection clause because it was not limited to display in newsracks, and found that the ordinance was limited by the knowledge requirements contained in the statute after which it was modeled.


OUTCOME: The court upheld an ordinance prohibiting display of adult publications in newsracks without blinder racks, but they could only be required when front page contained matter harmful to minors. To the extent the trial court's ruling was predicated solely on the finding that harmful matter inside a publication provided a basis for blinding the cover, it was erroneous, and remanded the issue for a determination of whether the front page was harmful.

People v. Superior Court (O'Donnell)


No. H013870.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


49 Cal. App. 4th 194; 47 Cal. Rptr. 2d 333; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1159; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9138; 95 Daily Journal DAR 15772



November 30, 1995, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted February 29, 1996 (S051089), Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1354.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 40 Cal. App. 4th 943.)] Opinion filed October 29, 1997 not for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Monterey County, No. SC941871, Richard M. Silver, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The People's petition for writ of mandate is granted and the writ is issued, ordering the superior court to vacate its judgment and restore all counts and allegations originally charged against defendant to the superior court calendar to be either tried or disposed of in some other appropriate manner. The superior court shall conduct further proceedings in accordance with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution sought a writ of mandamus to compel the Superior Court of Monterey County (California) to vacate its striking of a prior serious felony conviction as part of a plea agreement, arguing that the court lacked authority to strike the conviction under Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)-(i).


OVERVIEW: When the lower court struck a prior serious felony burglary conviction as part of a plea agreement on an auto theft charge under Cal. Veh. Code § 10851(a), the prosecution sought a writ of mandamus, arguing that the lower court had erred because it had no power to dismiss or strike prior serious felony convictions in the furtherance of justice on its own motion in light of the three strikes law, Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)-(i). The court held that although the lower court could dismiss or strike a prior serious felony conviction allegation, it could not dismiss or strike that conviction where it had been admitted. However, requiring prosecution approval for the lower court's striking or dismissal of a prior conviction for sentencing purposes violated separation of powers. Thus, the lower court vacated both the plea agreement and the lower court's striking of the prior conviction.


OUTCOME: The court vacated both the plea agreement in the case and the lower court's striking of the prior conviction, finding that the admission of the prior conviction removed the court's authority to strike but holding that the requirement of prosecutorial approval for the court's action violated separation of powers.

 

744 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Drew


No. B089318.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


49 Cal. App. 4th 180; 47 Cal. Rptr. 2d 319; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1167; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9181; 95 Daily Journal DAR 15795



November 30, 1995, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified December 14, 1995. Review Granted March 14, 1996 (S050984), Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1529.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 40 Cal. App. 4th 811.)]


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. VA026808, John A. Torribio, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The conviction and findings are affirmed. The sentence is vacated and the matter is remanded to the trial court for a new sentence hearing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, which struck a qualifying "prior" and imposed a sentence of nine years imprisonment on respondent for possession of codeine, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350, instead of imposing a 25-years-to-life sentence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state charged respondent with possession of codeine, Cal. Health & Saf. Code § 11350, and alleged respondent to have suffered four prior felony convictions. In a bifurcated trial, a jury found respondent guilty and then found true the four priors, as alleged. At the sentence hearing, over the state's objection, the trial court "struck" one prior in the interest of justice and then "converted" it into a state prison prior. The trial court then imposed the upper three-year term for possession of codeine, doubled it, and added one year, consecutive, for each of the three "state prison" priors for a total sentence of nine years. Appellant sought review. The court affirmed the conviction and findings, but vacated the sentence. In doing so, the court held that a trial court has no authority, on its own motion, to dismiss a qualifying "prior" in the interest of justice. The court explained that the absence of such power is not a violation of the separation of powers doctrine. Lastly, the court held that, in respondent's case, a 25-years-to-life sentence would not constitute cruel or unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed respondent's conviction for possession of codeine, but vacated the sentence imposed by the trial court. The court found that the trial court, in sentencing respondent for possession of codeine, had no authority, on its own motion, to strike a qualifying "prior" in the interest of justice. The court remanded the matter to the trial court for a new sentence hearing.

In re Andrew B.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the father, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) terminating dependency jurisdiction over three minor children and granting their mother sole legal and physical custody.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, the father, sought review of an order terminating dependency jurisdiction over three minor children and granting their mother sole legal and physical custody. The court found that appellate courts must independently review the record when appointed counsel in juvenile dependency appeal brought as a matter of statutory right submits a brief that does not argue against the client, but advises the court that no issues were found. The court concluded that there was no cognizable issue for appeal and affirmed the trial court's order.

Briggs v. City of Rolling Hills Estates


No. B083200.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


40 Cal. App. 4th 637; 47 Cal. Rptr. 2d 29; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1150; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8977; 95 Daily Journal DAR 15675



November 28, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BC076837, Aurelio Munoz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homeowners appealed an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, California, which granted summary judgment to defendant city on plaintiff's claims under the federal Civil Rights Act, 42 U.S.C. S. § 1983, that a neighborhood compatibility ordinance was void for vagueness.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff homeowners applied for a variance to construct four additions to their residence. The application was approved, but defendant city discovered a non-approved patio and deck that violated a city ordinance. Defendant's planning commission conditioned final approval of the addition on removal of the unapproved structure. Defendant's city counsel affirmed the decision. Plaintiff filed suit alleging three causes of action under the federal Civil Rights Act, 42 U.S.C. S. § 1983. In granting defendant's motion for summary judgment the trial court held that the ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague and plaintiffs' failure to file an action for administrative mandamus to challenge the city's determination barred plaintiffs' claims under the federal Civil Rights Act. On appeal, the court affirmed. In requiring the design to "respect the existing privacy of surrounding properties" the ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague. Plaintiffs' exclusive remedy was judicial review of the city's decision by administrative mandamus under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5. Plaintiffs' failure to pursue this remedy resulted in the administrative action becoming final.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed summary judgment in favor of defendant city and against plaintiff homeowners in plaintiffs' suit under the federal Civil Rights Act. The neighborhood compatibility ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague, and plaintiffs' exclusive judicial remedy was an action for administrative mandamus to challenge the city's determination.

Garcia v. Superior Court


No. G017206.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


49 Cal. App. 4th 143; 46 Cal. Rptr. 2d 913; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1149; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8935; 95 Daily Journal DAR 15469



November 22, 1995, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted February 29, 1996 (S050827), Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1352.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 40 Cal. App. 4th 552.)]


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 94WF2416, Richard L. Weatherspoon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: A motion to strike an allegation under the three strikes law based on ineffective assistance of counsel survives Custis v. United States, supra, 511 U.S.     [128 L.Ed.2d 517, 114 S.Ct. 1732] and Proposition 8. The petition for writ relief is granted. Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the lower court to vacate its order denying petitioner's motion, and ordering it to determine whether the matter should be set for hearing. We express no opinion on the merits of the motion. This court's previously issued stay order is dissolved upon finality of this decision.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which refused to entertain his motion to strike a prior conviction before his trial for drug possession and meant that he would be subjected to the "three strikes" law if he was convicted for drug possession.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner pleaded not guilty to possessing a small amount (35 milligrams) of heroin, under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350, denied allegations he suffered two prior convictions within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code §§ 667(d), 667(e)(2), and faced a potential prison term of 25 years to life, under the "three strikes" law. Petitioner filed a motion to strike one of the priors, alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. The lower court refused to entertain the motion. Petitioner filed a petition for writ of relief, which the court granted. The court held that a motion to strike an allegation under the three strikes law based on ineffective assistance of counsel was the proper procedural vehicle in the state court to test an allegedly unconstitutional prior conviction, although the same might not be true in federal court. The motion did not violate Proposition 8, Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(d), because it did not affect the standard for determining the prejudicial effect, under state law, of the introduction at trial of evidence that was inadmissible under California law. A peremptory writ of mandate was issued directing the lower court to vacate its order.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the lower court to vacate its decision because a motion to strike an allegation under the three strikes law based on ineffective assistance of counsel was proper in state court.

 

748 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


In re Marriage of Hoover & Shaw


No. A067284.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


40 Cal. App. 4th 433; 46 Cal. Rptr. 2d 737; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1129; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8867; 95 Daily Journal DAR 15305



November 20, 1995, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied February 29, 1996 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Offical Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1363.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. FL3155, Michael B. Dufficy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged an order from the Superior Court of Marin County (California) that restrained her from moving with her child and that granted primary custody of the child to respondent father.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mother and respondent father shared joint legal and physical custody of their child. A job change by appellant's new husband necessitated a cross country move, but the trial court restrained appellant from moving with her child and granted primary custody of the child to respondent. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that it was the burden of appellant as the moving parent to show not only that the move was necessary, but that it would be in the child's best interest. The trial court applied the proper standard in determining the child's best interest under Cal. Fam. Code § 3011 when it assessed each parenting plan. It was free to treat sibling unity equally with any other factor that might be important to the child's welfare and did not abuse its discretion in finding that the move was not in the child's best interest. Appellant did not have her right to travel unconstitutionally impinged upon because the order did not prohibit her from moving. The court concluded that the trial court properly modified the custody order in favor of respondent because the reality of the move necessitated a change in the original joint custody arrangement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial courts order restraining appellant mother from moving with her child and granting primary custody of the child to respondent father because the trial court did not abuse its discretion in determining that the move was not in the child's best interest.

 

749 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Williams


No. B087667.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


40 Cal. App. 4th 446; 46 Cal. Rptr. 2d 730; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1128; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8870; 95 Daily Journal DAR 15301



November 20, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BA063943, Charles H. Horan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As corrected, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which convicted him of first and second degree murder and recommended a life sentence without the possibility of parole.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted appellant of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187 and second degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187. At a penalty trial, the jury recommended life without the possibility of parole. Appellant challenged the conviction, arguing that the trial court erred in refusing voluntary manslaughter instructions, that the first degree murder should be reduced to second degree murder, and that his life without possibility of parole sentence was unconstitutionally disproportionate. The court affirmed, holding that no substantial evidence of provocation or heat of passion existed and that the trial court properly refused to instruct the jury on voluntary manslaughter. The conviction for first degree murder would not be reduced because it satisfied the three premeditation factors of plan, motive, and a planned method of killing. Appellant planned by bringing a loaded gun, his motive was revenge, and there was evidence of a planned method of killing. There was nothing disproportionate in the sentence because the California legislature authorized the death sentence for murders such as appellant's intentional killing of two unarmed, bound victims.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions, holding that the trial court did not err in refusing to instruct the jury on voluntary manslaughter because no evidence of heat of passion existed. The conviction for first degree murder was adequate because it satisfied the three premeditation factors of plan, motive, and a planned method of killing. The sentence was not disproportionate because the law allowed for capital punishment in such cases.

 

750 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Williams


No. D022227.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


40 Cal. App. 4th 429; 46 Cal. Rptr. 2d 728; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1124; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8829; 95 Daily Journal DAR 15266



November 17, 1995, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] March 14, 1996 the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Offical Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. SCD103592, William D. Mudd, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the matter remanded to the superior court with directions to permit Williams to withdraw his plea of guilty should he choose to do so within 30 days following finality of our decision, and for such further proceedings as may be required.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted defendant of petty theft with a prior, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 484, 666, after the trial court struck a "two strikes" allegation pursuant to a plea bargain the court made with defendant. The state challenged the court's authority to strike the allegation sua sponte, and argued that the sentence was void as an illegal sentence bargain.


OVERVIEW: The state appealed a judgment convicting defendant of petty theft with a prior, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 484, 666, after the trial court struck a "two strikes" allegation pursuant to a plea bargain the trial court made with defendant. The state alleged that the court did not have the authority to strike the allegation on its own motion, and that the sentence imposed was a void, illegal sentence bargain. Reversing the judgment of the trial court, the court agreed that the sentence imposed was void because it was an illegal sentence bargain. Cal. Penal Code § 667(g) prohibited the trial court from using prior felony convictions in a plea bargain where a guilty plea was exchanged for a judicial promise related to sentencing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment convicting and sentencing defendant and remanded to cause to the trial court with directions to permit defendant to withdraw his plea of guilty should he choose to do, and for such further proceedings as may have been required. The trial court was without authority to strike an allegation on its own motion, pursuant to a plea bargain it made with defendant.

 

751 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Patton


No. D021949.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


48 Cal. App. 4th 1769; 46 Cal. Rptr. 2d 702; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1125; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8824; 95 Daily Journal DAR 15259



November 14, 1995, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted February 15, 1996 (S050625), Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 979.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 40 Cal. App. 4th 413 and 44 CAl. App. 4th 1128.)]
Opinion filed December 22, 1997 not for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. SCD101675, Norbert Ehrenfreund, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We affirm Patton's conviction and the true findings made on his alleged priors. We modify his sentence to reflect the proper three strikes term under section 667, subdivision (e)(2)(A)(ii). (§ 1260.) The trial court is directed to prepare an amended abstract of judgment to reflect the correct sentence of 25 years to life plus a 1-year consecutive term for the prison prior, and to forward a certified copy to the Department of Corrections.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his sentence for possession of cocaine base ordered by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) under the three strikes law set forth in Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)-(i) (1994). Defendant argued that one or both of his prior convictions should have been stricken because the 10-years-to-life sentence that was substituted for the 25-years-to-life term mandated by § 667 still constituted a life sentence.


OVERVIEW: After being found guilty of possession of cocaine base, defendant was sentenced under the three strikes law set forth in Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)-(i) (1994) as a result of two prior burglary convictions. The trial court found that the mandated 25-years-to-life term for defendant's third strike was cruel and unusual punishment and imposed a term of 9-years-to-life plus 1 year for a prison prior, for a total 10-years-to-life term. On appeal, defendant claimed that one or both of his prior convictions should have been stricken because the remedy for the constitutional violation that occurred still constituted a life sentence. The court disagreed and held that the statutorily mandated 25-year-to-life sentence did not violate the constitution and that imposition of a term less than that mandated by the clear language of the three strikes law was an unauthorized sentence. The court reasoned that the mandated sentence was not disproportionate to the crime charged because defendant was being punished as a recidivist felon whose probation report showed a continuous criminal history for at least nine years. The sentence was modified to reflect the proper three strikes term under § 667.


OUTCOME: Defendant's sentence for cocaine possession was modified to reflect the proper three strikes term. The court concluded that the mandated 25-year-to-life term for defendant's third strike was not cruel and unusual punishment even though he was a youthful, nonviolent repeat offender.

 

752 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Re-Open Rambla, Inc. v. Board of Supervisors


No. B083097.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


39 Cal. App. 4th 1499; 46 Cal. Rptr. 2d 822; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 1094; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8679; 95 Daily Journal DAR 15003



November 9, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied January 24, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 517.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. WEC138790, Diane Wayne, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homeowners appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of defendant city that defendant county owned the road in question. The road was blocked by earth movement and later closed by the county. Plaintiffs sought to re-open that road but ownership of the portion which was within defendant city was in question as to who would be responsible to re-open the road.


OVERVIEW: A road, in the city of Malibu and earlier in the county of Los Angeles, was blocked by earth movement and closed by defendant county. Plaintiff homeowners wanted the road re-opened. However, there was a question as to who owned the portion lying within defendant city. Defendant city filed a motion for summary judgment on the ground that it neither owned nor controlled the road because following its incorporation, it had specifically rejected the road in question in its street system. Defendant county filed a motion for summary judgment on the ground that governmental ownership and control of the road were automatically transferred to defendant city on its incorporation. Plaintiff homeowners filed a motion for summary judgment. The trial court granted defendant city's motion for summary judgment. The court reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment because according to the court's interpretation of Cal. Sts. & High. Code §§ 980 and 1806, defendant city owned the governmental interest in the road and as such, defendant county's right, title, and interest in the portion which lied within the boundaries of defendant city vested in defendant city upon its incorporation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment in favor of defendant city which determined that defendant county owned the road, which plaintiff homeowners sought to re-open. The court held that defendant city owned the governmental interest in the road when defendant city incorporated. Thus, the road was part of defendant city's road system and it would be responsible for re-opening the road.
