People v. Hill


No. C018905.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


37 Cal. App. 4th 220; 44 Cal. Rptr. 2d 11; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 719; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6034; 95 Daily Journal DAR 10288



July 31, 1995, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts I through IV and VII.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified August 11, 1995. Review Denied November 16, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 6982. Certiorari Denied April 29, 1996, Reported at: 1996 U.S. LEXIS 2875.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court of San Joaquin County, Nos. SF062075A and SF061832A, Bernard J. Garber, Judge



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to award defendant 18 days of presentence conduct credit; as modified the judgment is affirmed. The trial court is directed to prepare an amended abstract of judgment and to forward a certified copy thereof to the Department of Corrections.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Municipal Court of San Joaquin County (California), which convicted him of possession of cocaine and petty theft with a prior theft related conviction and enhanced his sentence under the "three strikes" law, Cal. Penal Code § 667.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession of cocaine and petty theft with a prior theft related conviction, and his sentence was enhanced under the "three strikes" law, Cal. Penal Code § 667. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment but modified sentence to award defendant presentence conduct credit. The court found that Cal. Penal Code § 667(c)(5) did not abolish presentence credit and the "term" to be doubled included subordinate terms. Section 667(c)(5) specifically stated that credit did not accrue until defendant was physically placed in the state prison. The court rejected the state's contention that the statute meant that defendant could not take advantage of credits until he was in prison. The statute did not explicitly reference presentence credit, and the court could not read that language into the statute. If the legislature intended to eliminate presentencing credits, it could have done so when the ambiguity of the statute was presented before the law passed. Under Cal. Penal Code § 667(e)(1), the trial court properly doubled the base term for the drug offense and the subordinate theft term. "Term" was not limited to a single offense or the principal term.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of defendant for possession of cocaine and petty theft with a prior theft related conviction and the sentence enhancement, but the court modified sentence to award defendant presentence conduct credit. The statute limiting credits did not include presentencing. The trial court could double the base terms of both offenses because the legislative scheme did not limit "term" to a single offense.

 

785 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Chacon


No. B085237.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SIX


37 Cal. App. 4th 52; 43 Cal. Rptr. 2d 434; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 708; 95 Daily Journal DAR 10105; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5924



July 27, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified August 24, 1995. Review Denied November 2, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 6778.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. CR18681, Michael L. Duffy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The convictions for attempted kidnapping and false imprisonment (counts VII & VIII) are vacated. A stay of sentence for escape, assault with a deadly weapon, extortion, and false imprisonment of a hostage (counts I, II, IV, and VI) is imposed. The trial court is directed to send an amended abstract of judgment to the Department of Correction designating the following: count III as the principal offense with the upper term of four years, such term to be served consecutive to count V with a one-year enhancement for personal use of a dangerous weapon. As so modified, the judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged their convictions in the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California) for aggravated kidnapping, escape by means of force and violence, and lesser-related offenses, on sufficiency of the evidence grounds. Defendants also claimed that impermissible multiple punishments were imposed in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 654, and that the penalty for aggravated kidnapping was cruel and unusual punishment.


OVERVIEW: Defendants captured a librarian at knifepoint and used her as a shield to effect their escape from a juvenile facility in a truck they extorted from a guard. Defendants were convicted of aggravated kidnapping, escape by means of force and violence, assault with a deadly weapon, false imprisonment, and extortion. They received a sentence of life without parole for aggravated kidnapping, and various determinate sentences were imposed to run consecutively to the life term. Defendants appealed on evidentiary and constitutional grounds. The court held that sufficient evidence existed to support the sentence of life without parole. The victim suffered "bodily harm" when defendants choked her unconscious and stabbed her, and was exposed to a "substantial likelihood of death" when she was used as a hostage and forced to join their escape. The life sentence was not cruel and unusual punishment, because of the substantial risk to human life posed by kidnappings, and the heinous nature of defendants' crime. With the exception of vacated lesser-included offenses, and two sentences stayed as impermissible multiple punishments under Cal. Penal Code § 654, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Defendants' convictions were confirmed because sufficient evidence established that their victim suffered substantial "bodily harm" and was exposed to a "substantial likelihood of death." The aggravated kidnapping penalty was not unconstitutional due to the heinous nature of the crime and defendants' individualized culpability, but a stay was required of the impermissible multiple punishments for escape and extortion.

 

786 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Calderon v. Kane


No. A067726.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


36 Cal. App. 4th 1663; 43 Cal. Rptr. 2d 480; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 687; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5765; 95 Daily Journal DAR 9780



July 21, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied October 19, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 6411.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Napa County, No. 380906, Walter H. Harrington, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a complainant and associated individuals, sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of Napa County (California), which granted a motion filed by respondents, a writer and associated individuals, to vacate the dismissal without prejudice of a defamation action and to enter judgment on the arbitration award with attorney fees and costs.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, a complainant and associated individuals, challenged the trial court ruling, which concluded that dismissal of appellants' action for defamation triggered finalization of the arbitration award under Cal. R. Civ. P. 1141.20. The trial court granted a motion by respondents, a writer and associated individuals, to vacate the dismissal without prejudice and to enter judgment on the arbitration award with attorney fees and costs, and ordered that judgment be entered in accordance with the arbitrator's award. The court affirmed the grant of respondents' motion to dismiss and the trial court's order as to the arbitrator's award. The court did not see any point in forcing the parties to go through an unnecessary and unwanted trial, especially when a fundamental tenet of arbitration was avoidance of the court system. Thus, the court condoned an approach that safeguarded the rights of all parties to a trial following compulsory arbitration, while at the same time, to attempt minimal interference in the arbitration process. The court also stated that appellants' arguments had no bearing on the issue on appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision to grant a motion to dismiss filed by respondents, a writer and associated individuals, and to enter judgment on an arbitration award in an action for defamation. The court did not see any point in forcing the parties to go through an unnecessary and unwanted trial, especially when a fundamental tenet of arbitration was avoidance of the court system.

 

787 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Hicks v. Superior Court


No. D023687.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


36 Cal. App. 4th 1649; 43 Cal. Rptr. 2d 269; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 689; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5784; 95 Daily Journal DAR 9753



July 21, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied October 19, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 6432.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 172389, Federico Castro, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The petition is denied. The stay issued on June 7, 1995, will be vacated when this opinion becomes final.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner requested a writ of mandate challenging the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied petitioner's motion to strike people's motion under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707 to try petitioner as an adult for murder. Petitioner alleged that § 707 was unconstitutional in that it violated his rights to equal protection and due process under the law.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner committed murder at the age of 14 and 1/2. The people moved under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707(e)(1) that he be tried as an adult. Petitioner moved to strike on grounds that § 707 violated his equal protection and due process rights. The motion was denied and petitioner requested a writ of mandate to challenge the denial of his motion. The court denied the petition and held that petitioner was not in a suspect class and that there was no express constitutional guarantee giving a minor the right to trial in juvenile court. The court held that § 707 was constitutional and that it was rationally related to a proper legislative goal. The court held that the rebuttable presumption included in § 707 did not impact a fundamental right and that § 707's imposition of presumption of unfitness for a 14 or 15 year old offender for the most serious crimes, including murder, was a proper exercise of legislative power.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's request for a writ of mandate to challenge the trial court's decision to deny his motion to strike the people's motion to have petitioner tried as an adult. The statutory rebuttable presumption that 14 or 15 years olds were presumed unfit for juvenile court when committing the most serious of offenses was rationally related to a legitimate purpose and did not impact any fundamental right.

 

788 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Burnett v. San Francisco Police Department


No. A066667.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


36 Cal. App. 4th 1177; 42 Cal. Rptr. 2d 879; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 662; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5594; 95 Daily Journal DAR 9541



July 18, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied October 18, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 6309.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 950363, William J. Cahill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In sum, all of appellants' challenges to SFPC sections 1070 and 1070.16 are without merit. The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizens challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondents city in a suit to review the constitutionality and enforceability of the San Francisco Police Code art. 15.2, § 1070.16, alleging it conflicted with Cal. Fam. Code §§ 6500- 6502 in fixing the age of minority and majority, and it was vague, overbroad, and violated due process.


OVERVIEW: The San Francisco Police Code art. 15.2 § 1070.16 precluded persons under 21 years old from entering cabarets after hours and required the owners to exclude them from the premises; Cal. Fam. Code §§ 6500- 6502 fixed the age of majority at 18 years. Appellant citizens sought declaratory and injunctive relief against respondent city alleging the ordinance was preempted by and conflicted with the state statute, vague and overbroad, violative of equal protection, and discriminatorily enforced. On appeal, the court held there was no direct conflict between the statute and the ordinance because the statute defined minority and majority while the ordinance merely regulated the minimum age for persons on the premises of cabarets after hours; the ordinance was not vague or overbroad because a reasonable and practical construction of it limited its application to nightclubs after hours and there was no actual facts to show that it could not be applied constitutionally; and plaintiffs were not denied equal protection because the distinction in the ordinance between younger and older adults was rationally related to a legitimate state purpose of protecting younger adults and the community.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment in favor of respondent city, finding the ordinance was not vague, overbroad, or in conflict with the statute because it was limited to regulating the minimum age of persons in cabarets after hours and appellant citizens offered no actual facts to show it was applied unconstitutionally; and the age distinction rationally related to a legitimate state purpose and not violative of due process.

 

789 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Stacy & Witbeck v. City and County of San Francisco


No. A066609.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


36 Cal. App. 4th 1074; 44 Cal. Rptr. 2d 472; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 658; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5564; 95 Daily Journal DAR 9400



July 17, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing August 16, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 796. Review Denied September 7, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 961-598, William J. Cahill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the order granting the preliminary injunction. Parties to bear their own costs on appeal. A petition for a rehearing was denied August 16, 1995, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 7, 1995.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city and county sought reversal of a preliminary injunction from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) enjoining the city and public utilities commission (PUC) from enforcing the PUC's order declaring respondent contractor irresponsible and barring it from public works bidding for five years. The trial court found respondent's claim did not violate San Francisco Admin. Code § 6.45.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city and county had awarded a contract for construction of a light rail station to respondent contractor but problems arose after a 10-day shutdown period was extended due to delays. After respondent filed contract claims for damages, litigation ensued. The public utilities commission (PUC) brought administrative action under San Francisco Admin. Code § 6.45, found that respondent had submitted a false claim, and declared respondent an irresponsible contractor barring it from the city's public works projects for five years. The trial court issued a preliminary injunction enjoining appellant from enforcing the irresponsibility order and found no violations governed by § 6.45. On appeal, the court reversed the injunctive order and found that appellant's code allowed declarations of contractor irresponsibility under § 6.45 and San Francisco, Cal., City Charter § 3.591, that respondent had a fair hearing, that respondent had submitted overage claims seeking more compensation, and that the PUC's determination did not conflict with the state's sole power to qualify and license contractors.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that enjoined appellant city and county from enforcing an order that declared respondent contractor irresponsible and barred it from public works bidding for five years. The court found that appellant's code allowed declarations of contractor irresponsibility, that the declaration did not conflict with the state's power to license contractors, and that respondent submitted overage claims pursuing more compensation.

People v. Glaster


No. B086215.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


49 Cal. App. 4th 1910; 45 Cal. Rptr. 2d 65; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 643; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5437; 95 Daily Journal DAR 9244



July 12, 1995, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted October 19, 1995 (S048283), Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 6394.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 36 Cal. App. 4th 785 and 40 Cal. App. 4th 1825 and 45 Cal. App. 4th 299.)]
Opinion filed January 13, 1998 not for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BA093776, Charles E. Horan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The matter is remanded to the trial court to permit the defendant to withdraw her plea if she desires to do so. If defendant elects not to withdraw her plea, the trial court shall sentence defendant to a minimum of six years in state prison.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant people sought review of judgment from Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that imposed an unlawful sentence on respondent convicted of sale of cocaine. The trial court, over the objection of appellant, struck a prior strike allegation within the meaning of the three strikes law pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)-(i). Appellant contended that the trial court had no authority to strike the allegation.


OVERVIEW: Appellant people sought review of judgment that imposed an unlawful sentence on respondent convicted of sale of cocaine. The trial court, over the objection of appellant, struck a prior strike allegation within the meaning of the three strikes law pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)-(i). Appellant contended that the trial court had no authority to strike the allegation. The court on review rejected respondent's contention that Cal. Penal Code § 667(f)(2) impliedly empowered the trial court to strike a prior conviction allegation. The court held that the trial courts, on their own motion, lacked the authority to strike, strike allegations in the interests of justice. The court found that the trial court did not have the discretion to strike a prior conviction. The judgment was reversed. The matter was remanded to the trial court to permit respondent to withdraw her plea if she desired to do so.


OUTCOME: The judgment that imposed the sentence on respondent convicted of the sale of cocaine was reversed because the trial court did not have discretion to strike a prior strike allegation.

 

791 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Sipe


No. C018599.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


36 Cal. App. 4th 468; 42 Cal. Rptr. 2d 266; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 610; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5184; 95 Daily Journal DAR 8712



June 30, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified July 25, 1995.
Certiorari Denied February 20, 1996, Reported at: 1996 U.S. LEXIS 1223.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court of San Joaquin County, No. TF016683A, James E. Cadle, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Municipal Court of San Joaquin County, which found him guilty of escape from an honor farm, and based on a prior conviction for a serious felony, sentenced him to twice the term, four years in prison, under the three strikes law, Cal. Penal Code § 667(e)(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was serving time at an honor farm for burglary, a serious felony under Cal. Penal Code § 1192.7(c)(18). Less than one month after the three strikes law, Cal. Penal Code § 667(b) through (i), was enacted, defendant escaped. He pled guilty to escape and admitted the prior felony conviction, which was included in an amended complaint. He was sentenced under Cal. Penal Code § 667(e)(1) to twice the term, four years in prison. Defendant claimed that the use of the prior conviction in sentencing was improper because it would have been a retroactive application of the statute. The court found that the use of an increased penalty in subsequent offenses for habitual criminals was based on defendant being a repeat offender and was not a penalty for the prior offense. Defendant argued that the law was vague, but the court found that it was clear in § 667(e)(1) that the enhanced penalty was twice the term provided for punishment. The court affirmed the conviction, holding that defendant's due process and equal protection arguments were without merit because the legislature had specifically intended to more severely punish repeat felons.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence under the three strikes law based on his prior conviction for a serious felony, even though the prior felony conviction occurred before the enactment of the law, holding that the three strikes law did not violate due process and the imposition of an enhanced sentence was not a dual conviction of defendant because the legislature had specifically intended to more severely punish recidivist felons.

General Motors Corp. v. City of Los Angeles


No. B073381.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


35 Cal. App. 4th 1736; 42 Cal. Rptr. 2d 430; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 583; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4892; 95 Daily Journal DAR 8383



June 26, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. BC012780 and BC037986, Barnet M. Cooperman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the matter is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion. Appellant shall recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff out-of-state automaker appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), in favor of defendant city. Plaintiff maintained that certain business taxes imposed by the defendant on out-of-state manufacturers pursuant to provisions of defendant's municipal code were discriminatory and violated both the United States and California Constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff out-of-state automaker made and sold products in defendant city. Plaintiff filed an action that sought a tax refund from defendant on the grounds that plaintiff was subjected to two taxes, a selling tax and a manufacturing tax, whereas a manufacturer that was based in defendant city and sold within the city's borders paid only a manufacturing tax. Plaintiff argued that the selling tax discriminated against out-of-city manufacturers and interfered with interstate and intrastate commerce in violation of U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3, and the California Constitution. The trial court granted judgment for defendant. The appellate court reversed. The court noted that a state tax on interstate commerce was invalid unless it: [1] was applied to an activity with a substantial nexus with the taxing state, [2] was fairly apportioned, [3] did not discriminate against interstate commerce, and [4] was fairly related to the services provided by the state. The court ruled that the selling tax was discriminatory per se, and also failed the "internal consistency" test, which evaluates whether, if applied by every jurisdiction, a tax would avoid impermissible interference with free trade.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment for defendant city. The court found that the selling tax imposed on plaintiff out-of-state automaker by defendant was discriminatory per se and interfered with the flow of interstate commerce. The court found that the tax failed to meet the "internal consistency" test, which asks whether a tax, if applied in every jurisdiction, would avoid any impermissible interference with free trade.

People v. Brown


No. D021277.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


35 Cal. App. 4th 1585; 42 Cal. Rptr. 2d 155; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 567; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4818



June 22, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR142377, Richard M. Murphy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from a judgement of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted defendant of second degree murder of a human fetus under Cal. Penal Code § 187(a) and assault by means likely to produce great bodily injury under Cal. Penal Code § 245(a)(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of second degree murder of a human fetus and assault by means likely to produce great bodily injury under. The victim delivered a stillborn baby and doctors testified that the causes of death were an abruption of the placenta and a blow to the baby's skull. While in the hospital, the victim told her mother, doctor, friends and police officers that defendant had hit her and kicked her in the stomach out of jealousy. At trial, the victim testified that she was the aggressor in a fight with defendant and that she accidentally hit her stomach on some boxes. On appeal, defendant contended that the trial court erred in refusing a jury instruction on the offense of manslaughter and abused its discretion by admitting prior inconsistent statements of the complaining witness. The court rejected defendant's claims, ruling that the crime of manslaughter of a fetus did not exist. The court also found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in admitting testimony regarding the victim's prior inconsistent statements pursuant to Cal. Evid. Code §§ 1235 and 770 because the victim's trial testimony conflicted with her earlier statements.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for second-degree murder of a human fetus and assault. The court found that the trial court did not err in refusing to provide a lesser-included offense instruction to the jury because the crime of a manslaughter of a fetus did not exist and that admission of the victim's prior inconsistent statements was not an abuse of discretion.

 

794 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Giannini, Chin & Valinoti v. Superior Court


No. A064229, A064816.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


36 Cal. App. 4th 600; 42 Cal. Rptr. 2d 394; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 623; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5299; 95 Daily Journal DAR 8996



June 20, 1995, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published July 6, 1995. December 14, 1995 the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976 Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 945569, William J. Cahill, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner law firm sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent former clients in petitioner's action for unpaid legal fees and respondents' crossclaim for malpractice and breach of fiduciary duty.


OVERVIEW: Respondent former clients retained petitioner law firm to represent them in litigation against insurer and others. At the time respondents retained petitioner, petitioner also represented another client for whom insurer paid the costs of representation. After learning of the potential conflict of interest, respondents refused to pay legal fees, and petitioner filed a complaint seeking recovery of the unpaid fees. Respondents filed a crossclaim alleging malpractice and breach of fiduciary duties. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of respondents based on petitioner's violation of Cal. Bar R., Prof. Conduct R. 3-310. Vacating the trial court's judgment, the court held that whether and when petitioner was obligated to obtain respondents' informed consent depended on whether and when petitioner was in a relationship with insurer that could have compromised petitioner's representation of respondent. That was a triable fact not properly disposed of by summary judgment. Providing guidance to the trial court, the court stated that petitioner would not be entitled to fees for any time period during which it breached its duties to respondents.


OUTCOME: In petitioner law firm's suit for unpaid legal fees and respondent former clients' crossclaim for malpractice and breach of fiduciary duty, the court vacated summary judgment for respondents. There were triable issues of fact as to whether petitioner violated its duty not to engage in representation that posed a conflict of interest without first obtaining respondents' informed consent.

In re Voeurn O.


No. G015961.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


35 Cal. App. 4th 793; 41 Cal. Rptr. 2d 549; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 513; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4172; 95 Daily Journal DAR 7119



June 2, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 31, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 5371.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. J-155924, Robert H. Keefe, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile challenged a finding of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) which found appellant gave false information to a police officer.


OVERVIEW: Appellant juvenile gave an officer responding to a call of suspicious juveniles loitering a wrong name and denied being in a gang. Three weeks later appellant was arrested for providing false information after police determined he lied about his identity and gang affiliation. The statute appellant was charged under prohibited any person from falsely representing his identity upon a lawful detention. The trial court denied appellant's motion to suppress evidence on the ground of unlawful detention, finding that appellant was not detained by the investigating officer. The trial court found that appellant gave false information to a police officer. On appeal, the court reversed, finding that on its face the statute relied upon by the state did not apply to appellant because he was neither detained nor arrested when he gave the false statement. The court gave deference to the statute as worded because of its clarity and lack of ambiguity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the finding that appellant juvenile gave false information to police where the statute the state prosecuted under required that appellant be detained or arrested when giving the false statement.

People v. Jenkins


No. B082024.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SIX


35 Cal. App. 4th 669; 41 Cal. Rptr. 2d 502; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 499; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4082



May 31, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. PC-476, Michael J. Duffy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California), which found that he was a mentally disordered offender within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 2962.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with murder, found incompetent to stand trial, and sent to a mental institution. He subsequently regained his mental capacity and pled guilty to voluntary manslaughter. Prior to his release from prison on parole, defendant was certified as a mentally disordered offender (MDO) pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 2962. He challenged his involuntary commitment required by the statute, contending that the MDO statute, as applied to him, was ex post facto. The trial court found that defendant was an MDO within the meaning of § 2962. Defendant appealed, and the court affirmed, holding that because defendant committed his crime more than a month after the effective date of the MDO statutory scheme, the MDO statutory scheme, as enacted and amended, did not violate the ex post facto clauses of the United States and California Constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's holding that defendant was a mentally disabled offender (MDO) because his crime was committed more than a month after the effective date of the statute, which did not violate the constitutional prohibition of ex post facto laws.

People v. Avila


No. B085319.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


35 Cal. App. 4th 642; 41 Cal. Rptr. 2d 484; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 503; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4075



May 31, 1995, Decided


NOTICE:  [**1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part IV.C.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified June 29, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BA075213, Patricia L. Collins, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed except in three respects. The kidnapping for rape sentence as to count 4 is ordered stayed pursuant to Penal Code section 654. The Penal Code section 654 stay as to the Penal Code section 667.8, subdivision (a) enhancement to the sentence under count 3 is vacated and set aside. The trial court is to impose the three-year Penal Code section 667.8, subdivision (a) enhancement. The trial court is to deduct one day of conduct credits. As modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his convictions for forcible rape and kidnapping with intent to rape from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), arguing that the trial court erred by failing to instruct the jury that movement that substantially increased the risk of harm to the victim was an element of the kidnapping with intent to rape.


OVERVIEW: The trial court convicted defendant of two counts of forcible rape and one count of kidnapping with intent to rape. Defendant sought review of his convictions contending that the trial court should have instructed the jury that movement that substantially increased the risk of harm to the victim over and above that necessarily present in the crime of rape itself was an element of the kidnapping with intent to rape charge. The court modified defendant's sentences and affirmed the convictions in all other regards. The court explained that judicial precedent relied upon by defendant, which was decided while the appeal was pending, was applicable and that the trial court's instruction did not comply. The court, however, concluded that the harmless error standard applied to the failure to instruct on a single aspect of a multi-element offense. Accordingly, because the overwhelming and uncontradicted evidence indicated that the instructional error did not contribute to the verdict., the court found that the error was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment with regard to defendant's sentences and affirmed the convictions as modified. Although the trial court's failed to instruct as to an element of the kidnapping offense, reversal was not required because the error was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt.

Camp v. Jeffer, Mangels, Butler & Marmaro


No. B079386.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


35 Cal. App. 4th 620; 41 Cal. Rptr. 2d 329; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 497; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4066; 10 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1147; 95 Daily Journal DAR 7143



May 31, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing June 29, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 603. Review Denied August 17, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 5077.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SC016259, James F. Nelson, Judge, * 


* Retired judge of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment and the order are affirmed.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants husband and wife sought review of a Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) summary judgment under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 437c for appellee law firm in appellants' wrongful termination of employment suit which ordered that appellants return certain documents to appellee.


OVERVIEW: Appellants husband and wife were terminated from appellee law firm and sued for wrongful termination of employment on various grounds. The lower court entered summary judgment to appellee under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 437c, ordering appellants to return certain documents to appellee. Appellants sought review from the judgment and order. The court found that appellants' at-will status under Cal. Lab. Code § 2922 precluded any breach of implied contract. Further, where there was no implied contract there was no breach of covenant of good faith and fair dealing. Nor was there any violation of public policy where lack of lawful qualification for appellants' jobs barred them from complaining that they improperly lost them. Also, the misrepresentation claim was meritless due to appellants' at-will status. Finally, the order that the documents be returned was proper where there was no abuse of discretion in finding attorney-client privilege.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, finding that because appellants were at-will there was neither an implied contract nor a covenant of good faith and fair dealing, and holding that no public policy was implicated where appellants were guilty of misrepresentations in obtaining their employment.

 

799 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Wilder


No. B079341.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


35 Cal. App. 4th 489; 41 Cal. Rptr. 2d 463; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 486; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4018; 95 Daily Journal DAR 6843



May 30, 1995, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of the indicated portions.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BA070994, Roosevelt F. Dorn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) judgment convicting him of felony sale or transportation of cocaine pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11352A, arguing that he had not been properly advised of the dangers of self-representation under the Faretta standard.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of felony sale or transportation of cocaine base pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11352A. Defendant then appealed, arguing that the failure to advise him pursuant to the Faretta test of the dangers of self-representation required reversal. The court found that defendant had voluntarily chosen self-representation at arraignment, waiving the counsel who had been prepared to accept appointment to represent him. Although there had in fact been no admonition concerning the dangers and disadvantages of self-representation, the court held that the standard of review to be applied was the reversible error standard. Thus, where defendant had sought to abuse the Faretta right by later changing his mind about self-representation and misusing public funds given him for his self-representation the court found that no reversible error had occurred where the evidence demonstrated defendant's guilt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, holding that although defendant's waiver of counsel was not necessarily knowing, it was voluntary, such that where defendant attempted later to misuse that right and to change his mind about self-representation no reversible error was shown.

 

800 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


In re Daniel W.


No. F021767.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


39 Cal. App. 4th 937; 41 Cal. Rptr. 2d 202; 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 467; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3839; 95 Daily Journal DAR 6557



May 22, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted August 17, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 5059.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 34 Cal. App. 4th 1792.)]
Review Dismissed January 18, 1996 and cause remanded to Ct. App., 5th Dist. (rule 29.4(c), Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 370. Order directing publication of the opinion is denied.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 77030, Jerold L. Turner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juvenile challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which found defendant guilty of carrying a concealed weapon, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12025(b), and possessing live ammunition without the written permission of his parents, in violation of § 12101(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant juvenile was arrested for violating a city curfew ordinance, and a search incident to the arrest revealed a loaded weapon in his pocket. Subsequent to adjudication on charges related to weapons and ammunition possession, defendant argued that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress. Defendant contended that the municipal ordinance at issue was unconstitutional. However, after applying a test of reasonableness, the court held that the ordinance was constitutionally valid. Reviewing the language of the ordinance and comparing it to similar curfew ordinances, the court held that the ordinance at issue was not unreasonably restrictive, as it prohibited "loitering" rather than mere "presence". The court also found that the ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague, as it provided fair notice as to what conduct was prohibited. Thus, the court affirmed the adjudication, as the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion to suppress.


OUTCOME: Defendant juvenile's adjudication of guilt on weapon and ammunition possession charges was affirmed because a search of defendant was incident to an arrest pursuant to a constitutionally valid municipal ordinance that established a curfew.
